(London:Edward Arnold, 2002).It was also during the 1800s that the development of SouthernENGLISH CONSONANTS 79slightly later date, New Zealand, South Africa, and the FalklandEnglish Vowels4.4 IntroductionDescribing the vowels of English is a much more complex task than doing thesame for the consonants. The main reason for this is the magnitude of variationand differences in the inventories of different varieties of English. The numberof consonant phonemes is the same in all varieties of English (24), and thedialectal variations are relatively small. When we look at the vowels, however,we see that the number of phonemes varies, and the phonetic realizations ofthese phonemes may differ from one variety to another. Thus, in order not tooverwhelm the primary readership of this book — students and practitioners ofapplied linguistics, TESOL (teaching English to speakers of other languages),and communication disorders in the United States - our main focus will beAmerican English. At the end of the chapter, we will also present a comparisonof American English with some other major varieties spoken outside the USA.4.2 Vowel Set of American EnglishThe vowel set of American English can be described with the following keywords. The list contains what are commonly described as monophthongalvowels, which are mostly flanked between obstruents:Symbols used in Key word Symbols used inthis book other publicationsfil beat (fi, /§/, /iy/)Af bit/76/ bait (/er/, /ej/, /ey/)Applied English Phonology, Third Edition. Mehmet Yavas.© 2016 John Wiley & Sons, Ltd. Published 2016 by John Wiley & Sons, Lid.Companion website: www.wiley.com/go/yavas/appliedenglishphonology3eENGLISH VOWELS 81Symbols used in Key word Symbols used inthis book other publicationsfe/ bet/e/ batJaf bus (/a/ in unstressedsyllables)707 pot (/a:/)fof cloth (/3:/)/of boat (/ov/, /ow/)7807 book/u/ boot (/uw/, /u:/)Although these vowels are commonly described as “simple,” we have tomention that /i/ and /u/ are slightly diphthongized (hence the symbols /ij/, /iy/,and /uw/, respectively, insome books and manuals), and /e/ and /o/ are even morediphthongized (hence the symbols /ej/, /ey/, /et/,and /00/ , /ow/, respectively,in some books and manuals).DiphthongsThe following three are the main diphthongs of American English:/a/ bite (/aj/, /ay/, fai/)favf bout (/aw/, /au/, /av/)fa/f void (/oy/, /of/, /ai/, /oy/, / 이 /, / 이 /)4.2.4 Phonetic properties of vowelsAs we saw in Chapter 1, in the description of vowels, tongue position plays avery important role. Accordingly, one of the important dimensions is related tothe part of the tongue involved, and the other is related to the height of thetongue in the production of a specific vowel. According to the former criterion,English vowels can be classified as:Front: /i/, /1/, /e/, /e/, /e/Central: /a/Back: /u/, 707, /o/, /2/, /a/As for tongue height, we have the following groupings:High: /i/, /1/, /u/, /o/Mid: /e/, /e/, /o/, />/in some publications, a separation between “high-mid” and “low-mid” is givento separate /e/ and /o/ from /6/ and /9/.Low: /xe/, /a/, 707B2 ENGLISH VOWELSFRONT CENTRAL BACKHIGH beat f u bootbits u bookMID (high-mid) bait ¢ a boat(low-mid) bet e oclothLOW bus abate a potFigure 4.7 American English vowels.If we put these two dimensions together, we obtain the vowel chart shown inFigure 4.1.Besides the tongue height and the tongue part involved, the vowels of Englishare also grouped according to the lip position. The binary split is between therounded vowels (/1, 0, 0, 2/), and the unrounded vowels (the remainder). Allrounded vowels of English are back; in fact we can make the following gener-alization: all non-low back vowels are rounded in English.4.2.2 Tense-laxIt is also customary to see another binary grouping between the “tense” and“lax” vowels of English. This issue requires more detailed attention, as we mayfind different rationales and classifications in different publications. In somemanuals, the tense~lax distinction is present to account for two vowels that areotherwise described identically. For example, vowels /if and /i/, accordingto the dimensions discussed above, will both be described as “high, front,unrounded” vowels; the same problem is present with regard to /u/ and /o/,because both are “high, back, rounded.” Similarly, the front and back mid vowels/e,¢/ and /o, 3/, if not separated as high-mid and low-mid, will end up beingdescribed identically. To solve these problems, tense and lax are introduced; thefirst member in each of these pairs of vowels is called “tense,” because (2) it hasa higher tongue position, (b) it has greater duration than its “lax” counterpart,and (c) itrequires a greater muscular effort in production (hence the term “tense")than the lax vowel. This phonetic definition, however, is not universally adopted.Rather, one finds a phonologically defined “tense-lax” separation more popularin the literature. This distributionally based classification is more useful, becauseit divides the vowels into two groups that are distinguished by the environmentsin which they occur. Also, as we will see later in Chapter 7, this division will playan important role in the stress rules of English.The following describes the tense~lax rationale in terms of the different kindsof syllables in which the vowels can occur. Since all English vowels can occur inclosed syllables (as shown in the list at the beginning of this chapter) this cannotbe used as a criterion. However, when we examine the vowels and diphthongsthat can occur in stressed open syllables, we find /i, e, a, 3, 이 u, at, av, 31/ (tensevowels); /1, €, @, 0, 4/ (lax vowels) are absent in such syllables. Another syllabletype that is said to favor the occurrence of tense vowels and, thus, generallyENGLISH VOWELS 음음rejects lax vowels is closed by /1/. This, however, is a somewhat complex issue,because the contrasts between the tense vowels and their closest lax counterpartsare generally lost before /3/ for many speakers of American English. We willhave more to say about this later. As for the syllable types that favor the occur-rence of the “lax” vowels of English, we can cite the syllables closed by /f/ or by/4/. To summarize the tendencies in mutually exclusive environments, we cansay that “tense” vowels are typically found in stressed open syllables (andsyllabies with /1/ coda), while “lax” vowels are found in syllables with /{/ or/9/ coda.This distributionally based phonological classification of tense—lax does comeinto conflict with the earlier mentioned phonetically based classification. Firstof all, both /o/ and /2/ are “tense” in the latter classification, while they wereseparated (“tense” for the former, “lax” for the latter) in the phonetic classifica-tion. Second, there will be a problem with regard to “duration,” which the pho-netically based criterion focuses on. While it is true that several of the lax vowels(/1, €, 6, A/) are short, /z/ is not. Indeed, this vowel has equal duration with, oreven greater duration than, typically long and tense vowels such as /a, 6, 0/.We will not go into further details in this introductory text and, following thewidespread usage, will utilize the “tense” and “lax” grouping as defined by theoccurrences in different syllable types.Now we can revise the vowel chart and incorporate all that has been said.Before we examine in detail the different subgroups of vowels and their dialectalvariation, we will look at some other characteristics that are relevant to allvowels.4.2.3 Nasalized vowelsThe words bead and bean form a minimal pair, and any native speaker ofEnglish can tell that the final consonants in these words are responsible for thecontrast. Besides this obvious fact, however, there lies another differencebetween these two words and that has to do with the vowel sounds. While bothvowels belong to the phoneme /i/, the phonetic manifestation of the vowel ofthe second word, bean, is nasalized and, phonetically, an [i]. This predictableallophonic rule of English is valid for all vowels; that is, English vowels (anddiphthongs) are nasalized when they occur before a nasal consonant.84 ENGLISH VOWELS4.2.4 LengthLength of vowels (and diphthongs) varies predictably according to the contextthey appear in. More specifically:(a) Vowels are longer before voiced consonants than before their voicelesscounterparts. Thus, the phonetic realization of the vowel /z/ in bag [beg]is longer than its realization in back [bek].(b) Vowels are longer before sonorant consonants than before obstruents.Thus the phonetic realization of the vowel /o/ in goal [gol] is longer thanits realization in goad [god].(c) Vowels are longer in open syllables than in closed syllables. Thus, the pho-netic realization of the vowel /e/ in bay [be] is longer than its realizationin bait [bet].We can combine the three rules above and say that we find a vowel longest inan open syllable (e.g. /i/ in knee [ni}); next longest in a syllable closed by asonorant consonant (e.g. /i/ in kneel [nil]); next longest in a syllable closedby a voiced obstruent (e.g. /i/ in need [ 피 이); and shortest in a syllable closed bya voiceless consonant (e.g. /i/ in neat [nit]).(0) Vowels are longer in stressed syllables than in unstressed syllables. Thus,the phonetic realization of the phoneme /i/ in the stressed (bold-faced)syllable of the word appreciate [a.piifi.et] is longer than its realization inthe following unstressed syllable.4.2.5 Vowels before /a/Earlier we mentioned that vowels are affected by the surrounding consonants,and this effect is much more noticeable with certain consonants, especially withliquids. In this section, we will examine the vowels before /2/. In most forms ofAmerican English some form of r-sound after a vowel is permitted. When thefollowing /4/ is in the same syllable (as in ear, cure, work, party), the voweltakes on some retroflex quality, which is commonly known as “r-coloring.”When this happens, several otherwise well-established vowel contrasts ofEnglish are neutralized (i.e. lost) with many speakers of American English. Forexample, the contrast between the two high front vowels /i/ and /1/ seems todisappear in words such as ear, fear, beard, pier, etc. The r-colored productionresembles neither /i/ nor /1/; it is somewhere in between (traditionally tran-scribed as [ij]). A similar situation can be observed between the two high backvowels /u/ and /0/ in words such as tour, mature, endure, and poor Ther-colored vowel is not identical to either /u/ or /v/. This phenomenon of neu-tralizations of contrasts continues with full force in the front and back midvowel series. For many speakers of American English, the r-colored vowel inMary, merry, and marry is the same, thus revealing a neutralization of theENGLISH VOWELSpier poorL 8e Mary herder [hada] 0€ pair merry pourEa marry acoir Oltaly fire ag) sour car alFigure 4.2 Vowels before the tautosyllabic /4/.contrasts between /e/, /e/, and /2/. As for the back vowels, words such aspork, bore, horn, and fork do not seem to reveal any distinction between /o/and /2/,as the /1/ has the effect of raising the /o/ toward /o/ (cf. morning vs.mourning). Similarly, with respect to the high back vowels, the contrast between/u/ and /u/ may be neutralized in words such as poor and cure. Some speak-ers even go further and neutralize the four back vowels /0, 2, u, 6/ before /a/in coversational speech (e.g. pour, pore, poor).Besides neutralizing the above-mentioned distinctions, r-coloring is pre-sent in the following two diphthongs: /at/ (e.g. fire, entire, inspire) and/ava/ (e.g. sour, devour). Finally, the central vowel schwa has two r-coloredmanifestations: [3] in stressed syllables and [2] in unstressed syllables (e.g.herder [hyde]). We summarize the r-colored vowels with the tautosyllabic/a/ in Figure 4.2.Before we end this section, we should mention that an additional neutraliza-tion is present in some varieties of General American, and especially in easternNew England, New York City, and Southern American, whereby /21/ may shiftto /ai/, when the vowel and the following /1/ are not tautosyllabic. In wordssuch as foreign, moral, forest, and horrible, the vowel shown with the ortho-graphic 9 may be pronounced as /a/, thus giving us 0390], [40491], [fasast],and [hajebl]. Note that this shift is not possible if the vowel is in the same sy]-lable as the following /4/, as exemplified in score, shore, organized, and storm.The following example, observed in its multiple occurrences with severalspeakers, makes the point succinctly. The word forehead may be heard as[fahed] or [fasad]; in the first rendition, [1] is the coda of the first syllable (tauto-syllabic with the preceding vowel), and thus is not lowered to [a] (not [fashed]).However, the second rendition, which has the lower vowel, [a], necessarily putsthe [2] in the onset position of the second syllable.4.2.6 Vowels before /1/The effect of /1/ on the preceding tautosyllabic vowel, although not as drasticas that of /1/, is still noticeable. As we saw in the preceding chapter, postvocalic/\/ is highly velarized (dark) in American English, and this has a retracting86 ENGLISH VOWELSeffect on the front vowels. As a result, we have a more centralized vowel in thesecond word of each pair below:/if meat - meal/1/ Mick- milk/e/ pay —pale/e/ bet—belt/e/ tack - taleThe effect of the postvocalic /I/ on the remaining vowels (central and back)and diphthongs is more of a raising and backing, but might be lessnoticeable./a/ dot- doll/s/ hut~hullfo/ so~sole/o/ foot - full/u/ food - fool/a/ might—mile/av/ bout - bowel{uf coin - 004In Southern states and in the Mid-Atlantic region the mergers of /ul/-/ol/(e.g. pool - pull) and /il/-/il/ (e.g. peel - pill) are common. Also reported is the/al/-/ol/ merger in California.4.3 Front VowelsHigh frontThe two American English high front vowels, /i/ and /1/, differ in height,length, and tense-lax dimension. The /i/ is longer, higher, and slightly diph-thongal (hence symbols such as /i:/, /ij/, /iy/ in some books). In the produc-tion, the highest point of the tongue is a little lower and centralized, and israised and fronted in articulation. This is most noticeable in the final position,as in see, and least noticeable before voiceless stops, as in feat, where the dura-tion is shortest. For some speakers, [i] may be in free variation with [1] in finalposition (e.g. city [siti/siti], happy {hepi/heepi]). The use of final unstressed[1] is most common to the south of a line drawn west from Atlantic City tonorthern Missouri, thence southwest to New Mexico.The vowel /1/ has several different phonetic manifestations; it may undergo“tensing” and be realized as [i] before palato-alveolar fricatives (e.g. fish [fif}). InAAVE and in Southern American English, /1/ tends to be lowered to [e] beforenasals (e.g. thing [060]). Also observed in the same region is the tendency thatconverges the front vowels to [1] (e.g. gater, kettle, daddy). Finally, we shouldnote the free variation of [다 with [a] in unstressed syllables (e.g. believeENGLISH VOWELS 87[baliv/biliv], kitchen [ktfon/kitfin]) and in suffixes -ed, -es, -est, as in tempted[tempt(o/nd], bushes [bofle/ Hz], longest [loyg(a/1)st].Mid frontThe difference between the mid front vowels /e/ and /e/ is similar to thatbetween the high front vowels /i/ and /1/; /e/ is longer, higher, and tense, and/e/ is lower, shorter, and lax. The diphthongal nature of the tense one, however,is more pronounced; this is more obvious in open stressed syllables, such as say,or before voiced consonants (e.g. game, grade) than before voiceless consonants(e.g. gate) or in weak syllables (e.g. create). A more monophthongal (or verynaxrow) diphthong can be found in the northernmost Midwest region (e.g.Wisconsin, Minnesota).Parallel to the tensing of /1/ to [i], the vowel /e/ may be realized as diph-thongal [e] before /|, 3/ (e.g. special [spefal], cf. spatial) in the South. This is alsoextended to contexts before voiced stops (e.g. bed, dead), and as a result thecontrast between /e/ and /¢/ is lost, and egg rhymes with vague. Besidesthe free variation that exists before a tautosyllabic [1], there is also a free variationbetween /e/ and /z/ (e.g. apricot, matrix); this exists as well in the negativeprefix, as in amoral, asymmetric. Similar to the southern variety, /e/ may beraised to /1/ before a nasal in AAVE (e.g. pen [pen] - [pin]).Low frontEnglish has one low front vowel, /#/, which has different realizations in differ-ent regions. In Eastern American, especially in some New England varieties(Boston), the lower and more back vowel [al is common (e.g. half [haf], rat [aat]).(in the South, a diphthongal allophone is frequently heard (e.g. glass [glers],bad [beerd].)Before an [3] plus another vowel, as in carry, Paris, Arabic, [@] occurs alongthe Atlantic, along the Gulf Coast, and in the South, but [e] occurs more fre-quently in other areas. This vowel can be diphthongal, especially before /], 3,k/, in the South (e.g. splash [spleer{], back [beesk]).4.4 Gentral VowelsThe central, low-mid, lax vowel of English is /a/ (e.g. bus [bas]}. This vowel isfound only in stressed syllables; in unstressed syllables, a higher vowel, [2]“schwa" [fwal, is the realization (e.g. around [esaond)).Before a tautosyllabic [4] in stressed syllables, as in nurse, her, etc., a slightlyhigher, r-colored vowel, [3], is found. Its corresponding unstressed version is[a]. These are the two r-colored vowels of the word herder [h3da]. When /1/ isintervocalic, as in courage, it may be represented as in [31904] to show that thevowel is not in the same syllable as /1/. In some such words (e.g. hurry, worry),88 ENGLISH VOWELS[aa], instead of [34], may be found along the Atlantic seaboard, throughout mostof Pennsylvania ([haui], [wani]). There are several different treatments of thesecentral vowels in different manuals with respect to the number of phonemes.Without going into these controversies, we will adopt the following in ourtranscriptions:[a] in stressed syllables (e.g. bus [bas]}}[a] in unstressed syllables (e.g. sofa [sofa})[3] in stressed syllables before a tautosyllabic [4] (e.g. bird [bs d]}[a] in unstressed syllables before a tautosyllabic [4] (e.g. father [fada))[33] in stressed syllables before a heterosyilabic [4] (e.g. courage [ksyed3))[34] in unstressed syllables before a heterosyllabic [4] (e.g. parade [pozed])4,5 Back VowelsLow backThe low back vowel in American English is /a/,as in father. While many speakersof American English make a distinction between /3/ and /a/, as in the pairscollar ~ caller [kal] - [213], cot - caught [kat] - [kot], Don - dawn [dan] - [don],many others do not make this distinction and use /a/ for both. This collapse of/a/ and /3/ is one of the most significant vowel mergers in American English.The areas where this merger dominates include Maine, New Hampshire,Vermont, northern Massachusetts, western Pennsylvania, Midland territory,and the American West (with the exception of the metropolitan areas of LosAngeles and San Francisco). The areas resistant to this merger are the inlandNorth (US side of the Great Lakes region), and most of the South and the north-east corridor (Providence to Baltimore). As mentioned in Section 4.2.5, themerger does not apply when the vowel is followed by a tautosyllabic /3/; thus,born and barn are always distinct (cf. orange [2yands] or [ajandz}). Some speakersaround southeastern Ohio and northern West Virginia have /a/ and /2/ neu-tralized before /t/, but not in other contexts. There are also speakers who limitthis merger before nasals (e.g. Don and dawn).Some books suggest that, in some areas, /a/ has two allophones: [a] and [p].The vowel [ 미 , which is heard mainly on the eastern seaboard, has slight liprounding and lies between [a] and [9]; it is not used by all Americans. For thosepeople who use it, the distribution is as follows: [a] occurs in both open sylla-bles (e.g. spa) and syllables closed by a sonorant consonant (e.g. car, prom), and[이 in syllables closed by an obstruent (e.g. hot, posh).Mid backThe relationship between the mid back vowels of American English, /o/ and/9/, is similar to that of their front counterparts, /e/ and /e/. The vowel /o/,like /e/, is somewhat diphthongized and has a movement higher toward theend in production in open syllables (hence the symbols /00/ and /ow/ in someENGLISH VOWELS 89books). It is monophthongal (or very narrowly diphthongal) in the northernmostMidwest (Wisconsin, Minnesota). As mentioned earlier, before a tautosyllabic/4/, the distinction between the two vowels is lost for speakers in the New YorkCity area and across the northern USA, the west of New England. In the Southand upper New England, however, the distinction is maintained. Thus, pairssuch as hoarse - horse and morning - mourning may or may not be homopho-nous depending on the region.High backThe high back vowels, /u/ and /v/, behave very similarly to their front coun-terparts, /1/ and /1/. The vowel /u/ is slightly diphthongal (hence the symbol/uw/ in some books). While /u/ is centralized in the southeastern USA (e.g.school, good), /u/ may undergo “tensing” and be realized as {u] before a palato-alveolar fricative coda, as in bush [buf], which rhymes with douche, As men-tioned earlier, the distinction between /u/ and /u/ is lost before a tautosyllabic/a/ (e.g. tour) or /1/ (e.g. pull - pool); a similar situation may be observed insome other words spelled with 09 (e.g. hoof, roof, root), where either vowel isacceptable. Finally, in unstressed syllables preceding another vowel, /u/ maybecome lax [이 (e.g. gradual [quedgual]).4.6 OiphthongsThe three diphthongs of American English, /ai, au, 21/, given in Figure 4.3, canappear in all word positions, are all stressed on the first vowel, and all end inahigh vowel. While the end points are pretty well established across the varietiesof American English, the first element of these diphthongs may show consider-able variation.° fat/: The most common beginning point for this diphthong is [a], but itmay shift to a more back [a]. In the southern USA, /ai/ becomes [a:] or [81] ifnot followed by a voiceless consonant (e.g. buy [ba:], miles [mazlz]). In south-ern Philadelphia and parts of New York City, one hears [a1] instead. In themid-land and South, it is often reduced to a monophthong before /1/ (e.g.fire [fa3)).e /o/: For this diphthong, the starting point is back lower-mid, and thetongue glides from /2/ toward /1/. The lips are slightly rounded for the firstelement and neutral for the second. In Pennsylvania, Maryland, andDelaware, /o1/ sometimes approximates to [01]. In AAVE and in the South,we normally get a monophthongized [2:] (e.g. 에 [o:1], foil [fo:1], boil [bo:]}). InOuter Banks islands off the coast of North Carolina, [ar] may be found, thusmaking toid and tide homophonous.« /av/: The starting point for this diphthong is normally not as front as thatof /ai/, but not as back as that of /a/, although the latter may be the caseacross the North from New England through the Great Lakes, and on into90 ENGLISH VOWELSFront Central Back[기MidLowFigure 4.3 American English diphthongs.Minnesota. In the South, [ao] and [zo] (fronting and raising of the nucleus)are common, while in Nebraska and Iowa [zu] predominates. Raising thenucleus is typical in Canada (e.g. out [avt]), but also found in North Carolina,coastal Maryland, as well as in some northern areas. In Virginia and to someextent in northern New England, Wisconsin, and Minnesota, the allophone[au] is found before voiceless consonants (e.g. house [haus], out [avt]). Alongthe southern coastal area, fai] may be found, making down and dinehomophonous [dam]. Monophthongization of this diphthong is found inPittsburgh (e.g. down [da:n]).Besides these diphthongs, in non-rhotic varieties (eastern New England, NewYork City, lower South), the targets with V + /3/ turn into centering diphthongs.Thus, we have the following correspondences:Rhotic Non-rhotic/i/pier [pra]{if/e/ pear [pea]/u/poor 10089]70//o/pour — [poe]/a/In the case of [ai], we get a prolonged [이] rather than [as] (e.g. car [ka:)).The remaining three diphthongs show the following centralizations:[an] fire [fara}13041] sour — [sava}[ota] coir [6988]ENGLISH VOWELS 91As we have seen, there is considerable variation in the vowels and diphthongsof American English. Most of these differences deal with the following twowell-known chain shifts: Northern Cities Shift and Southern Shift. These shiftsare systematic changes in vowel systems with a rotation of the entire system.That is, when a sound shifts to the place of another sound, the latter soundshifts as weil in order to ensure that the two sounds remain distinct enough toconvey meaning differences. The two chain shifts that are under way inAmerican English are described below.Northern Cities Shift is seen in western New England, New York State, north-em Pennsylvania, northern Ohio, Illinois, Michigan, and Wisconsin. A moreadvanced form of this shift is mainly concentrated in the larger metropolitanareas (Syracuse, Buffalo, Cleveland, Toledo, Detroit, Chicago, Madison). Thisshift, which is a clockwise rotation, is shown in Figure 4.4.Six vowels, / 2, a, @, <, 1, a/, are affected. The low vowels /a/ and /a/ aremoved forward and/or raised, and the others are moved backward and/orlowered. /3/ in thought moves down and starts to sound like the /a/ offather. The /a/, in turn, moves toward /e/, thus lock starts to sound likeJack. The /#/ in man is raised and fronted to [i*] and the word sounds like[mi?n]. The two non-low lax front vowels /e/ and /1/ undergo a move back-ward (and also lower in the latter}; thus, bet starts to sound like but. Finally,/af moves backward to /3/, making shut sound like shot.Southern Shift is found throughout the southern states, the Mid-Atlanticstates, and the southern mountain states. This shift, besides monophthongizing/ai/ (e.g. five [fav] ᅳ [fa:v)), affects the following six vowels: /i,1, e, 6 u, o/. Itrotates them as follows: front lax vowels /1, ¢, e/ move upward (e.g. him[him] — [him]; bed [bed] - [bid]; can’t [kent] -> [kent]}, and front tense vowels/1/ and /e/ ave lowered and centralized (e.g. beet [bit] + [bit]; bait [bet] -[bet]). Finally, the back tense rounded vowels /u/ and /o/ move forward (e.g.boot [but] — [but]; boat [bot] — [bat]). Also observed are the raising of /21/ to/oa/ (e.g. born [6014] = [boin]) and /aa/ to /o2/ (e.g. barn [6010] — [boun]).These are summarized in Figure 4.5.13110)Figure 4.4 Northern Cities Shift.92 ENGLISH VOWELSbootboatureqnice>co Sec rel1409Figure 4.5 Southern Shift.4.7 Sociophonetic VariationBesides these variable productions due to linguistic context and regional varieties,socially conditioned phonetic variation is also pervasive in vowels. Mendoza-Denton (1996) found that, in the speech of girls in Chicana/Mexicana groups inCalifornia, realizations of the vowel /1/ could be predicted by centrality ofmembership to a particular group.The influence of sexual orientation on speech patterns has been subject to sev-eral studies. Pierrehumbert ef al. (2004) examined the vowel production in gay,lesbian, and bisexual speakers in read sentences and found differences 10 theacoustic characteristics of vowels as a function of sexual orientation. Lesbian andbisexual women produced less fronted /u/ and /a/ than heterosexual women.Gay men produced a more expanded vowel space than heterosexual men.Munson et al. (2006) examined the acoustic characteristics of simple words.(Formant frequencies (F,, F, etc.} are resonance frequencies of vowels/diph-thongs associated with cavities in the vocal tract. A detailed account of formantsis given in Chapter 5.) Gay men produced lower /z/ and /e/ (Le. with higherF, frequency) than heterosexual men. Lesbian and bisexual women producedhigher /6/ (i.e. with lower F, frequency) and move back /o/ (with lower F,frequency) than heterosexual women. In perceptual experiments, investigatorsfound that men who produced low vowels lower (Le. with a high F, frequency)and back vowels more back (with a low F,) were more likely to be rated asgay-sounding than were men with the opposite characteristics. Women werelikely to be rated lesbian or bisexual if they produced higher low vowels (witha low F,) and back vowels more back (with a low F,).4.8 Won-US VarietiesIn addition to the differences we observed within the United States, the non-USvarieties show significant variations. Tables 4.1 to 4.4 show the differencesbetween American English and some other Englishes. The tables are intendedENGLISH VOWELS 93to provide some basics regarding the vowel (and diphthong) variations amongseveral varieties of English. However, they are neither comprehensive descrip-tions of all varieties of English, nor do they pretend to give the details of varia-tions within a single variety.It should also be remembered that the symbols in Tables 4.1 to 4.4 areabstract in that the use of the same symbol for a sound in two or more varie-ties does not mean the sound is identical in different varieties. For example,when we consider AmE, CnE, RP, ScE, IrE, and WeE, we see that all have thesame symbol /e/ for a word such as hate [het]. This may give the impressionthat the phonetic qualities are identical in all varieties. This is definitely notthe case. While the vowel is definitely diphthongal in RP, its degree of diph-thongization is very slight in AmE, or basically monophthongal in ScE, IrE,and WeE. The vowel /o/ is another case where the same symbol is used fordifferent qualities; in AmE, this sound is often diphthongal, whereas in ScE,IrE, and We, it is monophthongal. Besides these monophthongal/diphthon-gal differences, there may be other variations. For example, although we usethe same symbols /e/, /2/, and // for the words pet [pet], sat [set], and bus[bas], respectively, in AmE and in RP we realize that these sounds are differentin the two varieties. While the first two have higher tongue position in RPthan in AmE, the situation is the reverse for /a/, that is, it has higher tongueposition in AmE than RP.Table 4.4 Comparison of the vowels and diphthongs of American English with those ofnon-Caribbean varietiesAmE CnE RP ScE IrE WeE AuE NZEbeak i i I i i i i ipit i I i I i i 1 ihate @ e€ e e e e Al ALpet € € £ € 8 € @ @sat 주 we @ a 3 8 6 £bath @ Fed a a a a a a:bus A a A a a a a apot a G D 3 a 2 ᄆ Dcalm a a a a a a a aloft 2 a D 3 0 0 D Dbought 3/a a x 2 a 가 2: 2soak @ ° aw ° ° ° AU Avbook 8 u 0 a 8 B v vboot u u 0 H ce a Au Aubike al Al al Al at al al alabout au AU au Au aw au 200 evcoin 가 2 21 36 re 21 21 2194 ENGLISH VOWELSTable 4.2 Vowel and diphthong variations before /r/, and the [i- 1], [2 - While all vowels ofEnglish (except [s]) can occur in stressed syllables, many of these vowels revealalternations with an [a] in unstressed syllables in a morphologically related word.Stressed syllable with a full vowel Reduced syllable with [a]/i/ homogeneous [homodsinias] homogenize [hamadgenaiz]/i/ implicit [implisat] implication [umplakefan]/e/ rotate [1066] rotary [zota.i]/e/ perpetuate [papetfuet] perpetuity [pepatfuati]/e/ enigmatic [anigmeettk] enigma [anigma]/a/ — stigmata [stigmata] stigma [stigma]/o/ author [2020] authoritarian [a8ojetezian]/o/ photograph [fotagueef] photography [fetaguafi]confrontation [kanfyantefen]computation [kampjatefan]designation [dezagnefen]/a/ confront {kenfiant]/u/ compute [kempjut]/at/ design [dezam]We should immediately point out, however, that a vowel’s appearance in anunstressed syllable does not necessarily result in a reduced vowel [a]. It isperfectly possible for the English vowels to appear in full (unreduced) form inunstressed syllables (or in syllables with secondary stress, which will bediscussed in detail in Chapter 7), as shown in the following:/i/ labial717 — immplicit/e/ rotate96 ENGLISH VOWELS/e/ centennial/e/ sarcasm/a/ October/o/ causality/o/ location/0/ boyhood/u/ acousticianfat/ titration/ao/ outside/x/ exploitationThus, the unidirectional generalization to be made is the following: while areduced vowel is necessarily in an unstressed syllable, a vowel in an unstressedsyllable is not necessarily reduced.Although we have consistently used [a] in reduced syllables, it is notuncommon to find an [1] in people’s speech. That is, for a word such asimplication we can get [implikefen] as well as [umplekefan]. In general, [1] isfound before palato-alveolars (e.g. selfish [selfi{], sandwich [sendwitf], mar-riage [me4id3]) and velars (e.g. metric [metyk], running [aanty]). It should benoted, however, that the syllable structure is also a factor. The infiuence ofpalato-alveolar/velar consonants is more visible when there is tautosyl-labicity. For example, we tend to find [1] in topic [taprk], which is likely tochange to an [a] in a related word such as topical [tapakal], because the velar,[k], is the onset of the following syllable. Individuals should check their pro-nunciation of such syllables and transcribe the vowels accordingly. However,since reduced syllables are necessarily unstressed, and [a] cannot appear ina stressed syllable (but [1] can), we encourage our students to use [a] for suchvowels, for practical reasons.We should also point out that [a], besides having this relationship with theunstressed [1], has a special relationship with three other vowels, /i, 0, u/. Inunstressed syllables, the range of pronunciation values of these three vowelsextends to the central [a] area, as shown in the following:fi]falrecord [14220] [10620]denounce [dinaons] [0608005]eleven {ilevan] [alevan][01 fa]produce [010005} [paedus]romantic [jomeentik] [zameentik]protest [psotest] [paatest]fu] falregular [aegjula} faegjele]graduate [gyeedguet}] [guzedzeet]circular [5300 4120] [sokjela]ENGLISH VOWELS4.10 Full (Strong) Forms versus Reduced (Weak)Forms of Function WordsThe pronunciations of words reveal differences, whether we consider themin isolation (ie. citation form) or in connected speech. The latter is a veryfertile context for accommodating many changes, especially for unstressedmonosyllabic function words (free grammatical morphemes). The class offunction words includes auxiliaries, prepositions, articles, conjunctions,pronouns, and some adverbs. In connected speech utterances, such wordsare typically not the focus of information (they are unstressed), and thusthey readily lend themselves to reduction. The reduced forms are very com-mon in connected speech, and their under-use (ie. employing the strong(full) forms) quickly strikes the native speaker’s ear as unnatural. Also,learners who have no familiarity with these forms are likely to have diffi-culty understanding native speakers who use them regularly in connectedspeech. Thus, learners should be frequently reminded of this aspect ofEnglish phonology. The existence of strong and weak forms, although lim-ited to a few dozen words, is very significant since these are some of themost basic words of the language, with a very high frequency of use. As willbe obvious from the list below, some of these function words have more thanone weak form. While some of these variations are predictable (e.g. [da]before consonants as in the book [09 buk], and [이] before others as in theapple [이 zpal]), many others are far from being invariable. Also noteworthyare the cases where two identically spelt words behave differently. For exam-ple, while that as a relative pronoun, as in “You said that she ate,” is reducedto [Gat], the identically spelt demonstrative that, as in that boy, is not reduce-able, and is always pronounced as [det].Before we list the items in question, we would like to remind the readerthat the citation form in isolation is not the only context that the strong (full)forms of these words can be used in. They are also expected when these wordsbecome the focal point in the exchange; for example, when given specialemphasis, as in:A: “We can serve strawberries or grapes for dessert.”B: "1 think we should serve strawberries and grapes.”or to make a contrast (assertion), as in:A: 1 can’t finish this job by Tuesday.”B: “Yes you can.”While the reduced forms [an] and [ken] are the expected forms in normal runningspeech, in the above examples the words will be uttered in their full form, [end]and [keen] respectively, when they become the focus of the exchange.98 ENGLISH VOWELSStrong and weak forms of some common function words in English follow:Strong Weaka [ 아 fo] “a book” fe bok]that (rel. pr.) [bilz ...]It is worth mentioning that prepositions and auxiliary verbs have certainrequirements for their reduced forms. Thus, for example, the preposition “by”behaves differently in the following two sentences:(a) He walked by the other day.(b) He walked by the other route.The reduced form occurs only in (b) because the preposition is followed by anoun phrase (Le, by has an object noun phrase and thus is a “transitive” prepo-sition). The “intransitive” (with no object) preposition by in (a) is stressed anddoes not reduce.Auxiliary verbs are typically unstressed and are reduced or contracted, except:{a) when they occur with the negative particle not. In such cases, only one ofthem can reduce/contract, not both:“The game hasn't started” (not is contracted, has is not),“The game’s not started” (has is contracted, not is not).The following two are not possible:* “The [gemzant] started” (lias. is reduced and not is contracted);*"The game [azent] started” (both lias and not are contracted).(b) when they occur in final position:“Is she coming?” “She is”“Has she returned?” “She has”“Do they like it?” “They do”“Who willread?” = “John will”Finally, we should point out that reduced vowels are not restricted to functionwords only, and are found in lexical morphemes (nouns, verbs, adjectives,adverbs) too. These will be looked at in the next chapter.SUMMARYIn this chapter, we looked at the vowels and diphthongs of American Englishand their contextual variants. Since the dialectal variations in vowels are fargreater than those of consonants, the discussion was focused on the US varie-ties. Non-US varieties are given in summary lists. As was noted in the previouschapter regarding the consonants, knowledge of the highly rule-governednature of vowel variations is invaluable for practitioners in the fields of com-munication disorders and foreign language teaching.1001.ENGLISH VOWELSEXERCISESInsome words, the sequence represented by orthographical or has thephonetic realization [93], which may be shifted to [a1]. Crystal, The Cambridge Encyclopedia of the English Language(Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1995).The movement of English around the world began with the pioneer-ENGLISH VOWELS 103times as many English-mother-tongue speakers as the next mostiAcoustics of Vowelsand Consonants5.1 IntroductionThe aim of this chapter is to present information that will be helpful to studentsof applied linguistics, teachers of English, and/or speech therapists in theirassessment and/or planning of remediation. As such, it does not deal with thedetails of speech acoustics. Rather, it is intended to supply some basic knowl-edge (i.e. to provide literacy) concerning the spectrographic analysis andwaveform analysis of speech. In order to make a reliable assessment, we needan accurate and adequate description of the client’s speech. The affordablesoftware currently available makes such procedures a real possibility both intherapy centers and in classrooms. Since speech that requires remediationreveals patterns that are different from the “norm” (average speaker), theprofessional who works with remediation may compare the speech of the clientto the “norm” to determine the amount of deviation. Waveforms and spectro-grams give us the ability to visually represent the audible signal in the study ofspeech sounds and allow us to look at speech, isolate segments for analysis, andexamine what goes on during an utterance. They are a very convenient meansof displaying the acoustic characteristics of speech in a compact form. Learninghow to interpret them is also relatively easy with practice. The data analyzedcan be used to monitor the changes in remediation and can guide the practi-tioner in adjusting the remediation plan. Spectrograms enable us to differenti-ate similar sounds within the major sound classes, and waveforms are usefulfor durational differences, distinguishing major sound classes and voicing.Before we begin making descriptive statements about these acoustic analyses,we need to alert the reader to the following important points. First, whilespectrograms and/or waveforms provide detailed information about severalaspects of speech and can be very helpful in assessment and remediation, itshouldbe noted that not all acoustically distinct phenomena are perceptually distinct.Applied English Phonology, Third Edition. Mehmet Yavas.© 2016 John Wiley & Sons, Ltd. Published 2016 by John Wiley & Sons, Lid.Companion website: www.wiley.com/go/yavas/ appliedenglishphonology3eACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 105Thus the practitioner must be able to pinpoint the information in the wave-forms or spectrograms that is pertinent for a particular case. It is also importantto emphasize the fact that perceptual cues interact with each other, and often,the coexistence of several cues is required to reliably identify an opposition withrespect to a single feature, such as height or backness of vowels. Second, it isimportant to emphasize that there are inherent problems associated with rec-ognizing words from acoustic information in a spectrogram. In addition to theexpected differences because of inter- and intra-talker variations, two otherissues ~ “linearity” and “invariance” - are particularly relevant. Although inour phonetic transcriptions we represent the sounds in a linear fashion, this issimply an abstraction. Speech articulations typically overlap each other intime, and result in sound patterns that are in transition much of the time. Theboundaries are blurred and the individual sounds can lose some of their dis-tinctive characteristics. Thus, one should not expect sounds to be linearlymapped on to waveforms or spectrographic displays. Also, as we will see indetail later in this chapter, characteristics of a given sound change in differentphonetic environments (e.g. the nature of adjacent segments, the length of theword, position of the word in a phrase, stress, rate of speech, and so on), andconsequently, one should not assume that sounds exhibit invariant character-istics in all contexts.Production of every sound sets a body of air in vibration. Two factorsinfluence the sounds produced. One of these is the size and the shape of theair. When a short and narrow body of air vibrates, it results in a higher pitchthan a body of air that is longer and wider. The other factor is related to theintensity of the sound. The wave forms created by these differences mayresult in simple (periodic) or complex (aperiodic) patterns, the formershowing regular vibrations, and the latter resulting in turbulent patterns.The sound source for vowels is always periodic. For consonants, it may beperiodic (glides, nasals} or aperiodic (fricatives), determined by thenarrowness of the consonantal constriction. Too much narrowing results inturbulent airflow.The three acoustic properties of speech sounds are frequency, time, andamplitude.¢ Frequency: Frequency relates to the individual pulsations produced bythe vocal cord vibrations for a unit of time. The rate of vibration depends onthe length, thickness, and tension of the cords, and thus is different for child,adult male, and female speech. A speech sound contains two types offrequencies. The first, fundamental frequency (£,), relates to vocal cord functionand reflects the rate of vocal cord vibration during phonation (pitch).The other, formant frequency, relates to vocal tract configuration.* Time: Time as a property of speech sounds reflects the duration of a givensound. For example, the duration of an alveolar fricative such as /s/ is .greater than the corresponding alveolar stop /t/.° Amplitude: The amplitude of a sound refers to the amount of subglottal(beneath the vocal cords) air pressure.106 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS5.2 Spectrographic AnalysisThe three acoustic properties mentioned above can be analyzed in a spectro-graphic display. The spectrogram is a three-dimensional graph of time, fre-quency, and amplitude. It analyzes a speech wave into its frequency componentsand shows variation in the frequency components of a sound as a function oftime. This allows us to see more detail regarding the articulation of the sounds.On a spectrogram, time is represented by the horizontal axis and given in mil-liseconds (ms). The vertical axis represents the frequency, which is the acousticcharacteristic expressed in cycles per second, or Hz. Each horizontal line on thisaxis indicates 1,000 Hz (or 1 kHz). The intensity (amplitude) is marked by thedarkness of the bands; the greater the intensity of the sound energy present atagiven time and frequency, the darker will be the mark at the correspondingpoint on the screen/printout. |If we look at the spectrogram of the monosyllabic word in Figure 5.1, we canmake the following observations. The vowel portion of the word, /1/, containsa series of thin vertical lines (striations) whose darkness varies with loudness.These lines represent the vocal cord vibrations. The space between the lines is ininverse relationship with the fundamental frequency (pitch), given in Hz. Forexample, with a fundamental frequency of 220 Hz (typical for the female voice),the vertical lines indicating voicing will be 1/220 second, or 4.5 ms, apart intime. The same vowel, with a fundamental frequency of 125 Hz (typical for themale voice), will have its vertical lines 8 ms apart in time. The vowel portionalso shows very clear horizontal dark bands. These are the resonance frequen-cies, called formants. The portion before the vowel /1/ represents the fricative/s/ with its high-amplitude frication noise in the higher frequencies. The por-tion after the vowel represents the stop /t/. This is shown with a gap, whichis the closure portion for /t/, followed by a vertical spike indicating the releaseof the final /t/. Each of these points will be made more explicit when we exam-ine the spectrograms of different classes of sounds.Adjustment of the spectrograph can create two different kinds of spectro-grams. Broad-band spectrograms have good time resolution, but blur frequency.Narrow-band spectrograms, although they are not as clear on the time dimension,have good resolution for frequency, and are generally the ones we see in booksand manuals.Figure 5.1 Spectrogram of sit.ACGUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 1075.3 Vowels and DiphthongsAs shown in the case of [sit], vowels have their frequency components groupedinto broad horizontal bands, called formants. Formant analysis is useful indistinguishing among sounds which have a clear formant structure; these arevowels, diphthongs, and some sonorant consonants. Different formants charac-terize different vowels and are the result of the different ways in which the air inthe vocal tract vibrates. Every time the vocal cords open and close, there is a pulseof air exiting the lungs, and these pulses act like sharp taps on the air in the vocaltract. The resonance patterns created by the vibration of this body of air aredetermined by the size and the shape of the vocal tract. Ina vowel sound, the airin the vocal tract vibrates at a number of different frequencies simultaneously.These are the resonance frequencies of the particular vocal tract shape. A formantfrequency, then, is a bandwidth containing a concentration of energy. Irrespectiveof the rate of vibration, the air in the vocal tract will continue to resonate atthese frequencies as long as the shape of the vocal tract remains the same. Theprecise acoustic makeup of each sound will differ for each individual speaker, butthere are certain core features that make it possible for us to identify the generalcategories. This is exactly the reason why we can recognize the same vowel pro-duced at different pitches by different individuals. Since vowels are associatedwith a steady-state articulatory configuration and a steady-state acoustic pattern,they are the simplest sounds to analyze acoustically. Customarily, formants arerepresented from the lower end of the spectrogram to the upper end. The clearlymarked dark bandwidth at the lower end of the spectrogram is the first formant,denoted as F,. The subsequent bands of similarly marked energy locations are thesecond (F,) and third (P,) formants. Vowel spectra have at least four or five obvi-ous spectral peaks. In general, the frequencies of the first three formants aresufficient to identify the vowels and the frequencies of F, and higher formantsvary among speakers because they are primarily determined by the shape andsize of the speaker's head, nasal cavity, sinus cavities, etc.Differences between the vowels of English can be explained in terms of thedifferent locations and widths of the formant frequencies. It is commonly statedthat there is a clear relationship between the frequency of the first formant andthe height of the vowel. This happens to be an inverse relationship; high vowelshave low first formants, and low vowels have higher first formants. As for thefront/back distinction, the frequency of second formants is often mentioned; wesee that the frequency of the second formant is much higher in front vowels thanin back vowels. However, the correlation between the second formant frequencyand the backness of the vowel does not seem to be as solid as the correlationbetween the first formant frequency and the vowel height. This is primarily dueto the fact that rounding in the case of back vowels can also affect the frequencyof the second formant. Thus, backness of a vowel is better related to the differ-ence between F, frequency and F, frequency. The simple formula is as follows:A vowel is more front when the difference between F, frequency and P, frequencyis greater than the same difference for another vowel. Whatever has been saidabove can be illustrated in the differences between beat and bet (see Figure 5.2).108 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTSFyfil More front vowel;greater F,-F, distanceFigure 5.2 Spectrogram of beat and bet.When we consider the “height” dimension, the expected inverse relationshipwith the frequency of F, is very clear. The sound /i/, which is higher than /e/,has its F, around 300 Hz, whereas the frequency of B, of /e/ is around 550 Hz.As for the difference between F, and F, determining the degree of backness,we can look at the two words again. The resulting numbers from F, minus F,frequencies for the vowels /i/ in beat (around 2,150 minus 300 = 1,850), andfor /e/ in bet (around 1,700 minus 550 = 1,150) quite clearly confirm that /1/ is,by nature, more front than /e/.To better understand how changes in the vocal tract shape result in differentformant frequencies, think of the “highest point of the tongue” dividing thevocal tract into two cavities (front/back). In a movement from the high frontvowel /i/ to the low front vowel /z#/, the body of the tongue is retracted,making the front cavity larger (this results in a smaller back cavity). Since theresonating frequency is higher for the smaller vibrating cavity than the largerone, such a movement will have the effect of lowering the resonating frequencyof the front cavity (the cavity that has become larger), and increasing thefrequency of the back cavity (the cavity that has become smaller). If we thinkthat the F, frequency is representative of the back cavity, and the F, frequency isrepresentative of the front cavity, we can understand how the changes in thevocal tract shape can result in the changes in formant frequencies. Thus, there isa gradual increase in F, frequency as we move from /i/ to /1/ to /e/ to /a/.A similar argument can be given to explain the lowering of both F, and F, fre-quencies as we move from /a/ to /u/. As the tongue root is raised toward higherback vowels, the back cavity enlarges, resulting in the lowering of the frequency ofF., Also, the highest point of the tongue is moving farther back from /a/ to /u/ ;and this creates a larger front cavity, which results in lowering the frequency of F,.While the above statements are quite solid for the gradual lowering of F, frequencyfor the back series, /a/ to /a/ to /o/ to /a/ to /u/, it is not so for the lowering ofthe frequency of F,. We consistently see higher F, frequencies for /0/ than /o/.This is primarily due to the more front production of /0/ than /2/. Table 5.1 showsthe formant frequencies of the first three formants in ten American English vowels.It should be remembered that these values are given as guidelines, not as absolutevalues. However, they are useful because they indicate the typical vowel patternsin relation to each other. For the front vowels, we see a gradual rise in values for B,and gradual decrease for E,. For the back vowels, we see a decrease in the valuesof F, and F, as we move from the lowest back vowel to the highest.ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 109Table 5.1 Frequencies of the first three formants in ten AmericanEnglish vowels (averages of three adult male speakers)Vowel F, F, F,i 270 2,200 2,900I 400 1,900 2,500£ 550 1,700 2,450Eg 670 1,670 2,450a 700 1,100 2,500a 600 870 2,450u 450 1,000 2,300u 320 850 2,250A 600 1,250 2,4503 500 1400 1,650100200ie 300Ou1 400 F,eues 500eee, e 3 600[1@ 700Sa vE,-F,Figure 5.3 Formant chart with frequency of F, (vertical axis) plotted againstdistance between F, and F, (horizontal axis).If the frequency of F, is plotted against the distance between F, and F,, a chartis obtained that strongly resembles the traditional vowel height charts. The single exception to this is [3], as in bird. Although its first twoformants are very similar to that of /x/, the frequency of its F, is very low; thiswill be clear in the discussion of /3/ later in the chapter.On the other hand, there are languages in which F, provides an important cuefor the vowel quality. For example, in languages with both front unrounded andfront rounded vowels (e. g. Swedish, German, Dutch, French), lip rounding forthe round vowels lowers all formants and moves P, close to F,; thus, the spacebetween F, and F, will be closer for [y] than for [i].The frequencies given in Figure 5.3 represent male speech. Men’s vowelstypically have lower formant frequencies than those of women, and those, inturn, have lower frequencies than those of children. This is due to the size of thevocal tract and hormonal differences; the larger the vocal tract, the biggerthe bodies of air contained. Since larger bodies of air vibrate more slowly, theformants will have lower frequencies in males. Also, the increased levels oftestosterone and dihydrotestosterone (DHT) during puberty are responsible forthe increase in size of the laryngeal cartilages. This increase is accompanied byincreased bulk of the laryngeal muscles and ligaments, leading to a drop in thepitch of the voice. Table 5.2 shows the comparison between men, women, andchildren (often, the FE, cannot be seen in children’s spectrograms). The frequen-cies are from Peterson and Barney’s (1952) data. The F, and F, values arerounded to the nearest 50.Although the formant patterns are the first and best markers in identifyingvowels, “duration” is also a very important parameter. As we saw in earlier chap-ters, several factors such as speaking rate, voicing of the adjacent consonant,utterance position, etc. can influence the duration of vowels. All of these factorswill be examined later in this chapter. However, before we do this, let us look atthe inherent durational differences among the vowels of English (Table 5.3).As we see, in general, tense vowels and diphthongs have longer durationsthan lax vowels. There is, however, a very clear exception to this principle: /z/,although a lax. vowel, has a longer duration than some of the tense vowels.ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 111Table 5.3 Duration of English vowels in stressed and unstressedsyllables (in milliseconds}Stressed syllable Unstressed syllablefi/ 118 (119) 75 (78)Af 75 (75) 58 (53)767 145 (136) 85 (78)767 103 (106) 73 (60)fe/ 152 (159) 82 (71)faf 99 (403) —faf ~ 55 (49)747 122 (126) 75 (75)fof 88 (85) 65 (61)707 156 (162) 92 (96)707 150 (148) 79 (72)707 146 (140) 85 (93)7867 178 (172) 120 (114)7207 199 (202) 121 (120)fal 264 (298) 132 (125)Note: The values given come from the averages of the productionsof a list-reading task from three adult native speakers (from lowa,Ohio, and Florida).
