One can easily seethe remarkable similarities between the two studies.The information regarding the vowel duration becomes critical when we havea pair of words in which the different vowel sounds do not present a big differ-ence in P, and F, frequencies. For example, if we have a pair of words such asbet [bet] and bat [bet], we may have a difference of 100-150 Hz for the firstformant frequency between these vowels, which is not very big. As for B,minus 본 , which determines the relative backness, the difference may be around200-250 Hz. In case these differences are not sufficient to distinguish the twovowels in question, the unmistakable difference in duration could be used tomake the separation, as /2/ is significantly longer than /e/.Diphthongs are different from monophthongal vowels in that the vocal tractchanges size and shape, due to the tongue moving in order to produce one vowelquality followed by another, and thus the formants are not horizontal through-out as they are in monophthongal vowels. The changes in the vocal tract shapefor diphthongs can be observed from the movements of the first three formants.The diphthong /at/ starts with a low back constriction and ends with a highfront constriction. Accordingly, F, rises as the constriction moves from back tofront, and F, falls as the constriction moves from low to high. In /av/,a low backconstriction moves to a high back constriction. As the tongue rises, F, falls; F,also falls because some movement of the tongue occurs farther back for /0/ than/a/; also, due to lip rounding of /0/ , all resonance frequencies are lowered.Finally, /or/ starts as a low back vowel and ends as a high front vowel.112 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS| Rising E, |Rising F,F,~F, distance smallertaped dewal Falling F, 이 Falling F, apFigure 5.4 Spectrogram of Brian Boitano bowed.As the tongue moves from back to front, F, rises; as the tongue moves from lowto high, F, falls. We can observe all the points made above in the spectrogram ofthe sentence Brian Boitano bowed [brain boitano baud] (Figure 5.4).5.4 Consonants5.4.4 ObstruentsObstruent sounds, the stops, fricatives, and affricates, are produced with so muchconstriction that the resulting noise causes the formant structure to be moredifficult to perceive on a spectrogram. The acoustic correlates of these consonants,however, can be determined via an examination of the formant movement ofadjacent vowels or sonorant consonants, as the movement from a constriction toa sonorant makes a formant structure visible on the spectrogram.StopsStops are characterized on a spectrographic display by silence (stop gap) orobvious signal weakening. This is the acoustic interval corresponding to thearticulatory occlusion, and it varies in duration depending on the prosodic con-dition. Because the vocal tract is obstructed, little or no energy is produced. Thisis followed by a burst of energy, as the closure is released.There are two dimensions that need to be carefully analyzed in distinguish-ing stop sounds. One of them is the separation of fortis from lenis (or, as morecommonly known, voiceless from voiced). Several indicators can help us toidentify the stops with reference to this dimension. These are:(a) Duration of the stop gap, ie. the silent period during the closure phase.Specifically, /p, t, k/ show longer closure duration than /b, d, g/.(b) Presence of a voice bar, i.e. a dark bar found at low frequencies (generallybelow 250 Hz) in a spectrogram. Except in intervocalic position, this is oneof the least reliable identifying characteristics of the stop sounds. As wesaw earlier, voicing is frequently absent in English /b, d, g/ in initial andfinal positions. Thus, this feature can reliably be used to separate /p, t, k/from /b, d, g/ only in intervocalic positions; /b, d, g/ will show the voiceACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 113bar that is indicative of voicing, whereas /p, t, k/ will not. The intervocalicvoicing can occur within a word (e.g. rubber), or when in connected speech,across a word boundary, as in “Harry gave it to me.”(c) Release burst indicated by a strong vertical spike. In general, we observea stronger spike for /p, t, k/ than for /b, d, g/. One qualification is in orderhere for the final position: as discussed earlier, final stops in English arenormally unreleased. In such cases, we will not observe any release burst.Velar stops are said to be more prone to non-release than bilabials oralveolars.(d} Duration of the previous vowel. This refers to positions other than theinitial position, where there is no vowel before the stop. In other positions,vowels and/or sonorant consonants are longer before /b, 0, g/ than before/p,t k/.{e) Aspiration. A lag of more than approximately 30 ms shows up as shortfrication noise (scattered marks after the release) before vowel formantsbegin, in initial /p, t, k/ of a stressed syllable.The other dimension in the identification of stops is the place of articulation.While the above criteria are useful in separating voiced (lenis) from voiceless (for-tis), we do not gather much information with respect to the place of articulation.In other words, what should we look for to distinguish a /p/ ora /b/ froma /t/ora /d/, or froma /k/ ora /g/? The following are helpful in this respect:(6) Formant transitions. Formant transitions (shifts) in consonant-vowel (CV)sequences reflect changes in vocal tract shaping (and thus changes in reso-nance frequencies) during stop-to-vowel transition. Vowel-to-stop (VC)transition is the opposite of the former. Frequency of the first formant ofthe vowel increases when stops are at the beginning of a syllable (CV), andfalls when they are at the end (VC); this is the case for all stops. While noinformation for the place of articulation can be gathered from F, transi-tions, F, and PB, transitions can tell us a great deal in this respect. In CVsituations, bilabials show upward movement of both F, and F, (morereliable before front vowels than before back vowels); the mirror imagesituation is revealed in a VC sequence. For a velar stop, F, and F, are closetogether just after the stop is formed (more reliable before back vowels;before front vowels, F, and F, have common origin). There will be a down-ward transition to a vowel ‘with a high F,. In cases of a VC, there is anarrowing (coming together) of the second and the third formants. Thecase of alveolar stops is probably the least straightforward; while weobserve a flat transition to a vowel with mid or high F,, there is a down-ward transition to a vowel with low F, in CV cases. In VC cases, there isvery little movement; small downward movement of the second formantand small upward movement of the third formant are expected. In CVCsituations what has been said for CV and VC repeat themselves and we getmirror images of the movements mentioned. These can be observed in thefollowing spectrogram (Figure 5.5).114 ACOUSTICS GF VOWELS ANG CONSONANTSFigure 5.5 Spectrograms of [beb], [deed], and [geg].The leftmost, [beb], shows a rising bow of F, of the vowel because it is comingfrom a bilabial; the transition from the vowel to the coda is marked with alowering of the F, (arrows a and b, respectively). In [dad], the F, transitionfrom the alveolar onset to the vowel is flat (expectedly around 1800-2000 Hz)which repeats itself in the transition from the nucleus to the coda /d/ (arrowscand d, respectively). Finally, in [geeg], we see that F, and F, are shooting fromthe same frequency which is the trademark of a velar onset; the mirror imageof this is observed in the coming together of F, and F, in the movement fromthe nucleus to the velar coda (arrows e and £, respectively).Locus (starting frequency /apparent point of origin). This is the point oforigin to which the transition of the second formant appears to be point-ing. F, and F, loci are described as an inherent property of the place ofarticulation for obstruents and nasals, although formant loci are not neces-sarily visible in the spectrogram. For a /b/, this is around 600-800 Hz; fora /d/, it is 1,800 Hz (mostly true, less so with high front vowel [i]). Toidentify a /g/, two loci, at 3,000 Hz and 1,300 Hz, are generaily noted.Release burst frequency. Bilabials are identified by bursts with a centerfrequency lower than the F, of the vowel (below 2,000 Hz). They show apattern that is diffuse and weak. Alveolar bursts generally have a centerfrequency that is higher than the F, of the vowel (2500-4000 Hz). Thepattern is diffuse and strong. As for velars, they display a compact andstrong pattern, and the bursts have a center frequency approximating tothe F, of the vowel.Aspiration (/p, t, k/ only). The length of time from the release of a stopuntil voicing begins for the following segment (ie. lag) presents consistentdifferences depending on the place of articulation of the fortis stop. Thetime interval includes the spike (the sound that is produced by the separa-tion of the articulators), a short frication noise after the spike, and the aspi-ration following it. Lag time measurements are often taken from the burstto the onset of the first formant of the following vowel. In case of voicedstops, the beginning of the second formant is preferred because voicingmakes it difficult to determine where the first formant of the vowel starts.As we move the place of articulation from the front to the back of themouth, stops tend to have greater voice onset time (VOT). Although this isACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 115a rather effective acoustic property for classifying the place of articulation,it should be remembered that it is just a tendency, and that it may bepossible to find an alveolar voiceless stop with a longer VOT than a velarvoiceless stop. VOT is also longer before high vowels than before lowvowels. These variations are probably an automatic consequence of differ-ences in degree of aerodynamic resistance to the existing airflow. Thehigher resistance offered by the high vowels delays the decay of pressureand thus the onset of voicing (Ohala, 2009). As mentioned in Chapter 3, theVOT for a syllable-initial voiceless stop varies somewhat systematicallydepending on the place of articulation and other segmental context.if we examine the spectrograms of the words speak, peak, keen, and cake(Figures 5.6 and 5.7), we can see these points clearly. In speak, the /p/ is not atthe beginning of a stressed syllable, since it is preceded by a /s/; thus we do nothave aspiration in the voiceless bilabial stop. The words peak, keen, and cake allhave voiceless stops at the beginning of a stressed syllable, and thus are aspi-rated. However, as one can easily see, the degree of the voice lag (aspiration) isdifferent in each case; shorter in peak, longer in cake, and the longest in keen.The reasons for these differences are as follows. Among the three, peak has abilabial stop whose expected lag is lower than that of the other two words thatstart with a velar stop. The difference between the two words that start withFricativeturbulence noise .F, and F, comingtogether{approaching velar)0 Release burstStop gap Stop gapFigure 5.6 Spectrogram of speak.Aspiration of /p/ Aspiration of /k/(a) peak (0) keenpik ko ion k e kFigure 5.7 Spectrograms of peak, keen, and cake.116 ACOUSTICS GF VOWELS AND CONSONANTSvelar stops (cake and keen) is due to the type of vowel that follows the voicelessstop. The aspiration in keen is longer than in cake, because in the former /k/ isfollowed by a high vowel (a vowel with lower sonority), while in the latter thestop is followed by a lower vowel {a vowel with higher sonority).Summarizing all the above, the examples confirm the statements madeearlier: that voiceless stops are not aspirated if they are not at the beginning ofa stressed syllable; when they are aspirated, the degree of aspiration is greateras we move the place of articulation from front to back (Le. bilabial, to alveo-lar, to velar). When a stop with a given place of articulation is followed byvowels of different height (different degrees of sonority), the amount of lagvaries; a stop before a high vowel has greater aspiration than when it is beforea low vowel. Flaps are different from their alveolar stop counterparts /t/ and/d/. In the production of stops, the vocal tract is blocked for about 50 ms, butin the flap, which is produced by a rapid throw of the tongue against thealveolar ridge, the duration is very short, about 20-30 ms; it is thereforethe fastest consonant.FricativesFricatives are produced via a narrow constriction in the vocal tract, whichcreates a turbulent noise (or “friction”). Such energy appears on a spectrogramas a scribbly pattern, without regular horizontal or vertical lines. The interval ofthe frication noise is greater in sibilants /s, z, |, 3/ than in non-sibilants /£ v, 8,5, h/, and the amplitude for frication noise is 58-68 dB (decibels) for the former,as opposed to 46-52 dB for the latter. The sibilants have places of articulation ator just behind the alveolar ridge. The airstream is funneled smoothly throughthe groove formed on the surface of the tongue blade and tip. As the air picksup speed it begins to tumble noisily. The tumbling, noisy air jet generally strikesthe edge of the upper incisors, or edge of the lower lip, and creates additionaledge or spoiler turbulence noise. These noises produced by sibilants are long,strong in amplitude, only a few decibels less than that of vowels, and markedby a rich, high-frequency noise spectrum.The non-sibilant front fricatives are made with labio-dental and interdentalconstrictions. The tongue tissue held against the teeth for the dental fricativescreates an abrupt, narrow constriction that generates a weak turbulence noisewhose energy is spread to very high frequencies. The noise is so weak thatlisteners often derive the acoustic clues for identification not from the noise butfrom the prominent consonant-to-vowel transitions of these sounds.If, for example, we make a comparison of /f/ as in fin with /8/ as in thin, wewould expect to find the following. Neither sound would create the fricationnoise as sibilants do (e.g. /s/ or /f/). Although the fricative noises are similarin both, the intensity range is lower in the former (3,000-4,000 Hz) than thelatter (7,000-8,000 Hz). What may also help to separate them are the formants ofthe neighboring vowels: F, {if visible) is below 4,000 Hz in /f/ and above4,000 Hz for /8/. One additional element may be the greater duration ofthe labio-dental than the interdental fricative. Finally, one might also find a littleACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS ᆞ 117higher F, locus for the interdental than for the labio-dental (Le. lowering of Finthe vowel following [8] and [6] than following [f] and [v]). Given all these smalldifferences, it is not surprising that very few languages demonstrate contrastbetween these sounds.Although we must use subtle clues to separate labio-dentals from interden-tals, the difference between alveolars and palato-alveolars is rather clear. If wecompare the /s/ of sin and /J/ of shin, we can easily pinpoint a very clear dif-ference of energy concentration; while the range is something like 4,000-8,000Hz for the alveolar, it is definitely lower, 2,000-6,500 Hz, for the palato-alveolar.The more forward constriction in the vocal tract produces a smaller vibratingbody of air in front of the constriction for the alveolar. This smaller mass of airhas a higher resonance frequency than the larger air mass in front of the palato-alveolar constriction used in /{/.Voiceless fricatives have longer noise segment duration and higher fricationnoise than their voiced counterparts. The lower frication noise of the voiced frica-tives is explained as a result of the total airflow available for producing turbu-lence at the constriction. Since the glottis opens and closes for vocal cord vibration,the airstream is interrupted, and the friction noise is not as loud in voiced frica-tives. Noise that occurs at the beginning of voiceless fricatives is similar to thebursts of /p, t, k/ (Le. aperiodic high-frequency noise during maximal closure).Voiced fricatives have formants produced by pulses from the vocal cords, asweil as more random energy produced by forcing air through a narrow gap.Since the airstream loses some of its kinetic energy to the vocal cord vibration,the frication noise in these sounds is not as loud as in their voiceless counter-parts. As a result, they have fainter formants.The articulatory constriction for a voiced fricative is not as tight as for a voice-less fricative, so as to ensure lower supraglottal air pressure while maintaininga sufficiently high transglottal pressure differential to allow the vocal cords tovibrate. This distinction between the relatively loose articulatory constrictionfor voiced fricatives and the relatively tight constriction for voiceless fricativesis another variable that reduces the amplitude of the fricative noise componentof a voiced fricative in comparison to a voiceless fricative. Whatever the causeof the reduction in amplitude of the noise component, it contributes to theperception of a voiced fricative (whether it is really voiced or not).Finally, some comments are in order for /h/, because its status is interpreteddifferently in different publications. The turbulent noise for this sound,generated at the glottis, is often accompanied by friction near the vocal tractconstriction for the adjacent vowel (/h/ is always a single onset). The constric-tion of the other fricatives involves the articulators (tongue, teeth, lips); theproduction of /h/, however, leaves these articulators free. Although it isnormaily treated as a fricative, /h/ is physically an aspiration. The signal forthis turbulent noise is very weak, and tends to be voiced (or breathy) intervo-calically, as in ahead or behind.The spectrograms in Figure 5.8 illustrate some of the points made above.Consider the patterns in (a) a fake, (0) sake, and (c) a shake. The differencebetween (a) and (b) (or (이) is so obvious that the greater frication noise for-118 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS/ffa non-sibilant Sibilants: strongweak turbulence noise turbulence noise(a) a fakeAlveolar: energyconcentrationJ 4,000-8,000 HzFricative duration greater Palato-alveolar: energythan /t/ of take concentration 2,000-7,000 HzFigure 5.8 Spectrograms of a fake, sake, and a shake.Stop duration is smaller호 tdthan /s/ of sake Aspiration~of a Release burstt e kStop gap Stop gapFigure 5.9 Spectrogram of a take.sibilants (/s/ or /f/) can be seen effortlessly. When we compare (b) and (c),we observe the frication noise concentrated at different frequencies;approximately 4,000-8,000 Hz for the alveolar /s/, and 2,000-6,000 Hz for thepalato-alveolar /J/.Now, consider the spectrogram for a take (Figure 5.9). This is given to showthe durational differences between stops and fricatives. If we compare the dura-tion of /t/ in a take with the duration of /s/ in sake, we can verify the greaterduration of fricatives than stops.Finally, if we consider the spectrograms of high and ahead (Figure 5.10), wecan observe the differences in two different phonetic manifestations of /h/.While it shows as a weak friction in high, it reveals itself as “breathy voice”intervocalically in ahead.ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 119(a) high, (b) aheadBreathy (voiced)Weak turbulence noise intervocalic /h/Figure 5.10 Spectrograms of high and ahead.(a) edging (b) etching은 dg 34 iyFigure 5.11 Spectrograms of edging and etching.AffricatesLike the stop consonants, affricates /t{/ and /dg/ require a stop-like closureperiod followed by a quick release into a friction noise. If we look at the spectro-grams of the pair edging and etching (Figure 5.11), we note the silent periodproduced by the consonant closure and the noise produced after the release ofthe consonant closure. The frication noise of an affricate differs from the fricationnoise of a simple fricative. It is strongest, and therefore darkest in a spectro-gram, near its beginning. In a simple fricative, the rise in the amplitude is moregradual. Temporal length of the fricative releases of affricates is shorter thanthe closure of the stop portions. Also noteworthy is the difference betweenthe voiceless and voiced members; as expected, the voiceless /t{/ is longer thanthe voiced /d3/.In Chapter 3, we stated that affricates, although phonetically a combinationof a stop and a fricative, function as single units in the sound system of English.The combined duration of an affricate is shorter than the duration of thecorresponding stop plus the fricative. This difference can be seen in thespectrograms of grav chip [guetfip] vs. great ship [guet{ip] given in Figure 5.12.120 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS(a) gray chip Affricate (b) greatship Stop + fricativets 해81661 itpFigure 5.12 Spectrograms of gray chip and great ship.5.4.2 Sonorant consonantsApproximantsLiquids and glides form the category of approximants. These sounds are madein such a way that one articulator is close to another without creatingenough narrowing to result in friction (sometimes referred to as “frictionlesscontinuants”). Since they do not have the complete closure like stops (andnasals), or the extreme narrowing of the fricatives, they do not exhibit formantdiscontinuities in their transition from the previous vowels and to the followingvowels. As sonorant consonants, they have formant structures but the formantsare less pronounced than those of vowels because of a slight obstruction placedsomewhere along the vocal tract. The shared characteristics in this groupinclude their low F, and similar durations for the transitions between vowelsand the consonant constrictions. Glides (or semi-vowels, as they are sometimesreferred to) reveal patterns very similar to, but markedly fainter than, highvowels. In /w/, we see that F, and F, are very close (as in / u/). In /j/,F, and F,are wide apart, resembling the situation of /i/ (low F, and high F,). A glidestarts with a vocal tract configuration similar to the corresponding high vowel,then changes shape for the vowel that follows it. A glide-to-vowel sequencing israther different from a vowel-to-vowel sequencing in that the transition from aglide to a vowel is faster than from a vowel to a vowel.Liquids have properties that are similar to both stops and glides (especially to/w/). They are quite rapid, like stops, but have resonant quality Gow F,) andtransition speed like those of glides. Liquids differ from /w/ in frequency of thethird formant; /w/ produces an F, that is usually so decreased in intensity that itis not visible in a spectrogram. The retroflex liquid /1/ is typically produced withthree simultaneous constrictions. These are lip rounding, a narrowing toward theback of alveolar ridge involving the tip of the tongue, and a narrowing in thepharynx due to the retraction of the tongue. All these serve to lower F, and thusthe third formant frequency of this sound is relatively low (below 2,000 Hz, thelowest F, in sonorant consonants), strong, and close to the second formantfrequency. While it is relatively easy to separate /a/ from /w/, /w/ and /!/ mayACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 121appear quite similar, because they have similar formant values for the first twoformants. The following two factors can be used to discriminate between the twosounds. First, the glide /w/ is formed with practically no constriction at all; theliquid /1/ is formed with a constriction of the tongue tip. As a result, the energyaround F, for /1/ is much higher than for /w/. Second, because of differentdegrees of constriction, the formants in and out of /w/ are continuous, whereasfor /1/, the formants at the vowel junctures show a slight discontinuity. This isdue to the contact made between the tongue tip and the alveolar ridge. The otherapproximants do not demonstrate such contact between articulators and so theirformant transitions change smoothly. To separate a /1/ from a /1/, one has toexamine the F,. In /1/, F, is much higher. In addition to these higher frequencies,the higher formants of the lateral are considerably reduced in intensity. The dif-ference between the “clear” and the “dark” /1/ lies in the timbre of their highfront/back vowel. In “clear /1/,” whereby the front of the tongue is raised towardsthe hard palate, F, and F, are farther apart, as we would expect in a high frontvowel. In “dark /I /, ” the tip contact remains at the alveolar ridge but the front ofthe tongue is depressed and the back is raised towards the soft palate, giving aback vowel or velarized resonance (ie. F, and F, are closer). Postvocalic final /1/may be like a vowel, making no contact, which results in an /u/-like formantstructure, However, as we stated in Chapter 3, in American English, /1/ morecommonly shows different degrees of “darkness” rather than the “clear” vs.“dark” contrast occurring in British English.To summarize, we give the following approximate formant frequencies (Hz)for the four approximants.w j 4 1clear darkBE, 200-300 200-300 300-350 200-400 350-550FE, 600-850 1,850-2,100 1,000-1,200 950~-1,400 650-850FE, 2,000-2,400 2,600-3,050 1,600-1,750 2,600-3,100 2,200~2,700NasalsNasals are formed by an oral closure accompanied by an open nasal passage.Both airflow and acoustic vibration pass through the open velar port into thenasopharynx and nasal cavities. Formants for nasais are not as dark as they arein vowels (and in approximants). The lower energy of nasals is due to the factthat the oral tract is completely blocked and the sound waves radiate from thenose. This acoustic radiation is known as nasal murmur, which can be seen onaspectrogram below 500 Hz.Nasals have a prominent low-frequency F,. There are two good reasons forthis. First, the nasal cavity is longer than the oral. Second, there is a strongattenuation of higher frequencies that are absorbed by the soft mucosal tissuethat lines the nasal cavities, and consequently, high-frequency energy gets“damped out.”122 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTSBesides the faint and a very low-frequency F, (200-450 Hz), the other possiblyvisible formant is F,, which is around 2,500 Hz. F, is generally not visible. Nasalsreveal the abrupt loss of overall energy. The nose is less efficient than the mouthin radiating the energy to the outside. One of the indicators of a nasal sound isa clear discontinuity between the formants of the nasal and those of adjacentsounds. The movement of the formants (formant transitions) in the neighbor-hood of nasals is very similar to those found in stops with regard to bilabial,alveolar, and velar articulations. Because nasals have lower amplitude and thushave problems of visibility of their formants in regular spectrograms, one canovercome this problem by increasing the intensity range displayed in thespectrogram by 15 dB (increasing intensity range brings up additionalresonances that are not normally visible). This makes the nasals more clearlyvisible, although it makes the vowels a little too dark.With respect to place of articulation, formant transitions associated with nasalsare very much like stops. For /m/, the bilabial nasal, the second formant has atransition pointing down. For the alveolar /n/, the second formant has a leveltransition. The transition for the velar points up, merging with the third formant.5.5 Putting it TogetherWe will review below several characteristics associated with certain sounds thathave been discussed by analyzing the spectrograms of eight words: (1) train, (2)kite, (3) pig, (4) happy, (5) basket, (6) ostrich, (7) scar, and (8) chicken (seeFigures 5.13-5.16).The eight words are equally divided into two groups in terms of their numberof syllables (four monosyllabics - scar, train, pig, kite - and four disyllabics -ostrich, basket, happy, chicken). Thus, looking only at the spectrograms andignoring the word labels below them, one can separate (1), (2), (3), and (7) as theformer (monosyllabics) because of their one vocalic nucleus formants, and isolate(4), (5), (6), and (8) as the disyllabics. Among the monosyllabics, scar is the onlyPalato-alveolar Frication noise for alveolarnoise pattern (4,000-8,000 Hz)0)~ Diphthongalvowel| oe HatrainFigure 5.12 Spectrograms of train and scar.ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 123fe) Release burst (3) Aspirated stopFo-F; comingtogether (velar)Voice barFork-like opening of F, andF, for the diphthong 7007Stop gapFigure 5.174 Spectrograms of kite and pig.00 (8)Faint and low-"frequency F, nasalShort lagchickenWeak turbulence Stop gap Palato-alveolar Stop gap Short lagfor dhl frication noiseFigure 5.75 Spectrograms of happy and chicken.(5) Alveolar frication noisebasket ostrich |F, and F, close Stop gap Alveolar frication noise Palato-alveolar" frication noisetogether for velarFigure 5.16 Spectrograms of basket and ostrich.124 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTSone that starts with a sibilant. Although all four words show frication noise tur-bulence at the beginning, only (1) and (7) are logical candidates for this target, asthe noise patterns of (2) and (3) are generalized to all frequencies (a typical patternfor aspirated stops, and thus for pig and kite), rather than the expected concen-trated pattern for /s/. This suggests that (1) and (7) are train and scar (seeFigure 5.13). Between the two, (7) is a better candidate for scar, as the fricationnoise is concentrated between 4,000 and 8,000 Hz, typical for alveolars. There ismuch more supporting evidence for this identification. The frication noise is fol-lowed by a stop gap and the following release, /k/, with a short lag (expected ofan unaspirated stop), which in turn is followed by a low back vowel (high F,, anda narrow space between F, and F,) and the r-coloring of the following vowel (thelowering of the third formant). Turning to (1), train, we observe the following: thepalato-alveolar nature of the frication noise (ie. affrication of [ty]) is evidenced bythe 2,000-8,000 Hz range of the noise pattern. Also, we note that the followinglong and rather diphthongal vowel /e/ (fork-like opening of the formants) andthe following faint and very low-frequency F, nasal fit the expected pattern.Words (2) and (3) are kite and pig. How do we know which one is which?While both are CVC words that start with aspirated stops and thus cannot bedifferentiated via this feature in the spectrograms, the features for the remainingparts make the choices rather clear. First of all, the lengths of the vowel nucleiare quite different. In (3), we see a shorter vowel (/1/ of pig). The nucleus of (2)is longer and diphthongal (/ai/ of kite starts with a low vowel followed by ahigh front vowel, and thus, has a fork-like opening). To make things furtherindubitable, one needs to look at the “coming together” of F, and F, right beforethe stop gap for the coda in (3). As discussed earlier, this is typical of velar stops(/g/ of pig). Both words are produced with their final stops released (note therelease burst after the stop gaps). This explains the visible voice bar in /g/ ofpig. Thus, (2) is kite and (3) is pig (see Figure 5.14).Turning to disyllabics, we again start with the frication noise. The only wordthat starts with a frication noise is chicken, and (8) is the best candidate for thisword, as the noise pattern for /t{/ is expectedly dark and goes down to 2,000Hz, which is typical for palato-alveolars. Word (4) shows some frication noise,but the frequencies and the intensity of it are far less than would be expected of/t§/; indeed, it is typical of /h/, which suggests that (4) is happy. There areseveral other indicators that support the identification of these two words: thevowels in the stressed syllables are of very different length, longer /z/ inhappy, and short /1/ in chicken. The portions immediately following the firstsyllable are expectedly similar (stop gap and short lag release for the unaspi-rated stops). The differences, however, are clear right after that. The secondsyllable of (4) has a longer vowel (/i/ of happy), as opposed to the short [a] ofchicken in (8). To add further support to this identification, we can look at thefaint and very low-frequency F, for the nasal ending of chicken (see Figure 5.15).The two remaining words, basket and ostrich, will have to be matched withspectrograms (5) and (6). This isa rather easy decision for several reasons. Word (6),which has two very obvious frication noises, is ostrich. The first one is /s /, withatypical 4,000-8,000 Hz alveolar pattern, and the second one is /t{/, with a lowerACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 125push typical of palato-alveolars. The same word shows some affrication for [ty]right after /s/. Still in the same word, the stressed first syllable has a longer vowelwhich is low back (note the F, and F,); the second syllable is unstressed (weakintensity and short duration). When we examine (5), basket, we observe the fol-lowing: unsurprisingly, no visible voice bar for the initial /b/. As discussed inSection 5.4.1, this is one of the least reliable identifying characteristics of the stops/», d, g/ except in intervocalic position. The stressed first vowel, /z/, is expect-edly long, and the frication of /s/, though not as strong as that of ostrich, is stillclear. The following sound, /k/, has its stop gap, which is followed by an expectedshort lag (unaspirated) release because it is at the beginning of an unstressed syl-lable. The formant transitions for the following vowel (F, and F, are close togetherjust after the stop, with a downward transition to a vowel with a high F,) clearlyindicate the velar place of articulation. Finally, we note the second syllable with arather short vowel followed by a stop coda (see Figure 5.16).5.6 Waveform AnalysisAnother frequently used analysis in speech production is waveform analysis.A waveform (also known as an oscillogram) is a bi-dimensional graph thatshows how the amplitude of some dimensions of sound changes over time.Among several acoustic properties that can be inferred from waveforms thisbrief account will focus on duration and intensity. In a waveform, the horizontalaxis represents the time and the vertical axis represents the amplitude. The zeropoint in the middle of the vertical axis shows the ambient atmospheric pressureand the movements going above (positive amplitude) or below (negative ampli-tude) this line represent alternations of the pressure above and below atmos-pheric pressure, as shown in Figure 5.17. Amplitude of a wave is equal to thePhonetic9080706050403020100-10-20-30~40-50-60-70~80-90Waveform (percentage)도 S & 3오고고 SE ELEEFLEEEEESESFigure 5.17 Waveform of /i/.126 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTSlargest deviation from average pressure. It is measured in decibels (dB) and isperceived as loudness auditorily. Naturally, the general amplitude of the wavewill depend on how loudly or how softly the person speaks or how close themicrophone is. However, different sounds have different amplitude when spo-ken in identical conditions. The highest point of the wave is called the “peak,”and the lowest part is called the “trough.”There are four basic types of waveforms and these are demonstrated inFigure 5.18 and with the words spider and times.Phonetic9080706050403020100-10~20-30-40-50-60-70-80 !-90 s Pp " drise ep oe ses 5 & F&F SP SFSe Eg PF sg FFF 개세sec) 7 PP oF oF OP gh oh oF oP oF 0” SWaveform (percentage)& &S CayS & &PF Ss> 시은 ~~Figure 5.18a Waveforms of spider.Phonetic .9080.70.605040.3020 .100-10 .-2030 .-40-50-70 .-80-90 :Waveform (percentage)come nByiy“GpSPP OSS SF FPS 주LLP IP LP LLP 00 02 09"SF L& 680660 WF PPSWE A 소조사노Figure 5.18b Waveform of Himes.ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS ANO CONSONANTS 127As one can see, there are clear changes in the waveforms that signify differentsegments of speech. There is no way to determine exactly which sounds aredisplayed in the different sections of the waveforms. As pointed out earlier, wecan only recognize duration, voicing distinctions, or broad classes of soundslike stops, fricatives, nasals, and vowel-like sounds.The periodic waveforms have cycles that repeat. These are typical for sono-rant sounds (i.e, vowels, nasals, liquids, and glides). The periodicity is due tothe fact that the source of all these sounds is the periodic vibration of the vocalcords. Although no sound is absolutely periodic, vowels show the periodicityfrom one wave cycle to the other. In the above waveforms, spider has [a1] and[ 히 , and times has [ai] and [m] displaying this characteristic. As expected [ai] hasgreater amplitude than the reduced vowel [a]. Figure 5.19 shows a complex (butrecurring) pattern of vibrations consisting of major peaks with a series of minorpeaks in between in a periodic wave; it is a wave graph of a section of the sound[i] 00666 than 50 ms of the total vowel duration of 190 ms) taken from the wordbeet. This complex pattern occurs ten times within the section that is shown inthe figure. However, each successive cycle of the wave is slightly different fromthe preceding one because the sound is not completely stationary.Random waveforms which are typical for noisy turbulence are found withfricatives and the aspiration phase of voiceless stops; they do not have a repeat-ing cycle, and therefore are aperiodic. As we see above in Figures 5.18a and5.18b, in fricatives [5] of spider and [z] of “times,” and the aspiration phase ofthe voiceless stop [t"] of times the pattern is chaotically up and down from thezero line.The closure phase of a stop, which shows up as a stop gap in a spectrogramis represented by a quiescent waveform. During this phase of voiceless stops,the waveform is said to be a straight line as in [p] of spider and [t*] of timesbefore the release of the closure. However, it is important to point out that,because of the background noise and echo during recording, the amplitude ofsilence never shows as zero in a waveform. If the stop has voicing, as in theintervocalic [d] of spider, then we see some pulses on the line.Finally, we look at the release phase of stops in the displays and see the ape-tiodic waveforms which dissipate very quickly ([p] of spider and [t"] of times).Such transient waveforms are a result of the air pressure that has built upbehind the articulatory closure and is released with an explosion.The relationship of the four waveform types with the classes of speech soundscan be summarized as follows:Periodic: vowels and sonorant consonantsRandom: fricatives and the aspiration phase of voiceless stopsQuiescent: closure phase of stopsTransient: release phase of stopsIn order to measure speech sounds, it is necessary to draw boundariesbetween adjacent sounds. Theboundary that will be drawn between [w] and the following vowel [6], and theone between the vowel and the following [I] will be a guess, guided by the waythe amplitude changes. In such situations, it is a good idea to use spectrograms in130 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTSconjunction with waveforms. Spectrograms are better for identifying segments,but actual measurements should be made with waveforms.As we see in these displays, it is less challenging to find the segmentation inthe spectrogram than in the waveform.Waveforms are frequently utilized in measurements of duration, as spectro-grams are not very good in giving precise information in the time domain. Thisis accomplished better with the expanded time scale waveform which is allowedin most systems displaying waveforms.One of the most commonly investigated durational features is that of VOT whichshows the elapsed time between the release of a stop consonant and the onset ofvocal cord vibration of the following segment. When we look at a waveform tomeasure VOT, the starting point, which is the release of the closure, is marked bythe change from quiescent to transient. The end point is marked by the change fromtransient to periodic waveform which indicates the onset of vocal cord vibration.Figure 5.22 shows the regular and expanded waveforms of the word “attack.”Phonetic 600 00090 :80 :70:6050:4030:20 :100.-10 :-30 :~50 |-60 :-70 :-80 : i90: ny kWaveform (percentage)9 oO & S SS Ss x S SS 8 8 &t(sec) & § Sa 토이투여 PP PG PEF SF ASSAM HSSe PP PPP Ss PP PF PL PPSPD _ PO. This is the starting point for VOTmeasurement. The end point is determined from where the waveform becomesperiodic, signaling the onset of vocal cord vibration for [@]. The end of the wordis marked with the change from this periodic wave to a transient one, which,expectedly, is the closure phase for final [k].In the following, we summarize the characteristics observable in waveformsof different groups of sounds.VowelsThey have periodic waves and have the highest relative amplitude (due to thegreatest opening of the mouth), and greater duration than the other sounds.The general shape of the waveform patterns and the fine detailed features arerepeated at regular intervals of time. The main difference between vowels andsonorant consonants is that the former is characterized with more jagged wavesthan the latter.Voiceless stopsThey are very easy to spot; silent (no amplitude) during the closure phase. Onawaveform, they appear as a flat line, usually followed by a burst. The burst isweakest with bilabials and highest with alveolars. Also, the waveforms of velarstops commonly show a double burst phenomenon which is not seen in bilabialand alveolar stops. Lag in waveforms exhibits a sharp spike (where the wave-form changes from quiescent to transient) denoting a release burst, and theonset of voicing is identified as the last extreme negative deviation from 0 thatprecedes the onset of regular voiced pulsation where the waveform becomesperiodic (Le. jagged and “saw-tooth” waveforms). Aspirated stops are followedby an aspiration noise, which shows as an aperiodic wave that follows the silentinterval and continues until the start of the following vowel. VOT is muchlonger in aspirated stops than in unaspirated ones. Figures 5.23 and 5.24 havethe waveforms of tick, and stick, respectively.As mentioned earlier, the amount of lag in aspirated stops changes withthe change in the place of articulation; the more back the place of articulation is,the greater the lag. This is shown in Figure 5.25 by comparing /p/, /t/,and /k/in pie, tie, and kite.The lag in [p"] is 63 ms, which increases to 71 for [t*] and to 80 ms for [k*]. Itis also important to note that the burst for the alveolar is the most intense andsudden. This is due to the agility of the tongue tip which can be controlled veryaccurately to produce rapid articulatory maneuvers.Voiced stopsThese are characterized by a periodic signal with small overall amplitude. Stridents have higher amplitude thannon-stridents. The latter is similar to voiceless stops, but without the followingrelease burst. /0/ has the least energy, /f/ a little more, and /s/ and /{/ havethe greatest energy, as seen in Figure 5.28/h/ is rather different in that it has very small random changes, which maybecome rather different intervocalically, as seen in Figure 5.29Voiced fricativesThey are characterized by random, rapid fluctuations superimposed on aperiodic wave. Finally, although nasals have the same places ofarticulation as the stops, they don’t have the aperiodicity of aspirated stops nordo they have the bursts that we see in stops.ApproximantsThey have vowel-like waveforms. /j/ and /I/ have smaller amplitudes thanneighboring vowels. With the exception of /1/, the others are just a way of begin-ning a vowel with slowly increasing amplitude. /]/ is rather different in that it ismarked with a clear increase in amplitude when the vowel begins. The lateralhas additional features due to the side passage of air. When /I/ is followed by avowel, the transition to the vowel occurs more rapidly and step-like (Figure 5.31).Finally, post-vocalic liquids are impossible to distinguish in waveforms asshown below in Figure 5.32.(a)Phonetic ;90 :80706050403020100~10~20-30-40-50-60-70~80Waveform (percentage)rc E e&Ne »NV& 8S @ Db S S S 8 & & © & pb CS) 도이 CS §6609 FP POO SF FF SES PPP PS(b)Phonetic9080706050403020100-10-20~30~40-50-60~76-80-90Waveform (percentage)©SStisec &‘s 1g!(다Phonetic -Waveform (percentage)s © ©우 ©€ PS EF S&S HF EEFEEEFP EEF HPP SFS060 FP PP PPP FFA PLE MOSFigure 5.31 Waveforms of ray, way, yay.(0)Phonetic9Waveform (percentage)11441 116 글으니 2 8 boreeooveocces-90:t(sec)Figure 5.31 (continued) Waveform of lay.(a)Phonetic ᆞWaveform (percentage)Qo-60:~70:~80 |~90 :t(sec)(b)Phonetic ;Waveform (percentage)=-90 :atsec) of(sec) PgFigure 5.32f iS € FS SF ES HS HLS FEF HSNg Ng oe No s Nea ANi i| :해 aPS PSE SK ESE SWaveforms of feel and fear.ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 1415.7 ContextAlthough we have talked about the characteristics of different sounds in acous-tic displays, as mentioned at the beginning of this chapter, there are no absolutecriteria that could uniquely define what a sound /X/ is in all phonetic environ-ments, even for a particular individual. Thus, the lay person’s assumption thatphonemes exhibit “invariance” can hardly be justified.Several factors influence both vowels and consonants. Earlier (see Table 5.3),we saw that the duration of vowels is different in stressed and unstressed syl-lables. Besides this, other variables are important too. One of the frequentlymentioned variables is the influence of the following consonant, because thesame vowel may be significantly longer before a voiced consonant than beforea voiceless consonant.The effect of the following consonant on the duration of the preceding vowel16 mentioned above. In fact, as we saw in Chapter 3, this effect is also observedin all preceding sonorant segments. If we look at the spectrograms of the pairssent ~ send, and kilt ~ killed (Figure 5.33), we can observe the differences in thedurations of the vowels and the sonorant consonants before voiced and voicelessstops. As we can see, the durations of the vowels and the sonorant consonantsare, expectedly, greater before /d/ than /t/.Fricatives exert an even greater influence over vowels than stops. For example,we get the following readings for the same vowel [a] depending on the natureof the following consonant. We found the following differences with the changein the following segment:but [bat] (vowel length before a voiceless stop: 104 ms)bud [bad] (vowel length before a voiced stop: 172 ms)buzz [baz] (vowel length before a voiced fricative: 210 ms)The length of the word, as well as its number of syllables, seems influential. Forexample, while we get a reading of 93 ms for the vowel [다 in pick, it goes downto 56 ms in picky and to 37 ms in pickiness.Position of the word in the phrase or in the sentence, the rate of speaking, thetype of word, either topic or comment, all influence the duration significantly.(a) sent - send (b) kilt - killedPruf pe5 —€ n t s eon dA.Figure 5.33 Spectrograms of sent ~ send and kilt - kille142 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTSFor example, the vowels of the two words dog [>] and man [e] change theirduration by between 20 ms and 65 ms in the following sentences:“The [dog] ((9]: 161 ms) bit the [meen] ([ee]: 175 ms)”“The [meen] ({z]: 155 ms) was bitten by the [dog] ({o]: 226 ms)”Similarly, ina listing situation, the last item has greater duration. If we comparethe following two sentences, we get different durational readings for peachesand oranges in their two locations:(a) Llike apples, oranges, and peaches.(b) 11166 apples, peaches, and oranges.For example, we get an average of 94 ms as the durational reading for the final [z]of oranges in sentence (a), which goes up to a 174 ms reading for the same soundin sentence (b). Pre-pausal (at the end of phrases, clauses, or sentences) stressedvowels seem to have the longest duration; this is diametrically opposed to vowelsin function words. As a result, the duration of a vowel in the former position maybe up to two or even three times that of the same vowel in the latter.Consonants can also be influenced by the contexts in which they appear. Wesaw earlier that the aspiration of the voiceless stops varied according to the fol-lowing segment. Specifically, there was a longer lag before a sonorant conso-nant (e.g. play [p*le]) than before a vowel (e.g. pay [pte]). As for their duration,fricatives are the consonants that are more consistently affected by the context.For example, we get readings of 196-231 ms for the /s/ of sub [sab], which goesup to 309-325 ms in final position in bus [bas]. Yet, when we place it at the endof the phrase take the bus, /s/ gives us a reading of 328-365 ms.Speakers adapt to various circumstances of communication and adjust theirproduction patterns accordingly. Thus, it is no surprise that speech style and rateinfluence the production, and we see a clear difference between slow, careful speechand fast, colloquial speech. While the former is characterized by a slower tempo,avoidance of reductions and/or deletions, and an attempt to make the produc-tion as distinct as possible, the latter is replete with deletions and reductions,and the durations of the components necessarily get shorter. These contrastingphenomena, which are known as hyperspeech (or “overshooting”) versus hypospeech(or “undershooting”), should not be conceived of as a binary split, but rather asa continuum (hence the terms “very slow,” “slow,” “normal formal,” “conversa-tional,” “fast,” “very fast”). Consequently, the different speech realizations and dif-ferent acoustic readings are the results of these varying production patterns.Example: Colorless green ideas sleep furiously (12-syllable sentence)very slow 2.0 syllables per secondslow 2.6 syllables per secondnormal formal 48 syllables per secondconversational 6.0 syllables per secondfast 8.0 syllables per secondvery fast 12.0 syllables per secondACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 1435.8 Practical Applications: Some ExamplesAs we noted at the beginning of the chapter, acoustic analysis is a very usefultool in applied disciplines. The increasingly affordable software on the markethas made its use viable in a clinical context and in foreign language teaching.The utility of acoustic analysis comes from the fact that it provides quantitativeand objective data on a wide range of speech parameters (e.g. nasalization,vowel quality, segmental duration, place and manner of articulation, VOT), andgreatly enhances the scope of auditory-based perceptual judgments of speech.it is particularly helpful in monitoring changes during remediation (clinicaland/or classroom context).Real-time spectrographic displays have been used in speech training withindividuals who have severe and profound hearing impairments. Althoughsome have expressed concerns about the usefulness of spectrograms in speechtraining, citing their complex and abstract nature, several investigators havepointed out their successful use with hearing-impaired adults (Maki 1980, 1983;Maki ef al. 1981) and children (Ertmer and Stark 1995; Ertmer ef al. 1996) withrespect to contrasts such as voiced~voiceless, durational differences, tongueposition for vowels, and differences in manner of consonant production. Ertmer(2004) examined how well children with normal hearing and children withimpaired hearing can recognize spectrographic cues for vowels and consonants,and the ages at which these visual cues are distinguished. Subjects’ trainingactivities involved instruction, highlighting of target spectrographic cues, match-ing of spectrograms by the children, and feedback on correctness. Results showedthat a variety of spectrographic cues were recognized with greater-than-chanceaccuracy at each age level and across both hearing statuses. On average, formantcues were recognized with greater accuracy than consonant manner features,making vowels, diphthongs, and vowel-like approximants easier to recognize.This saliency, Ertmer suggests, may be the result of a combination of greaterduration, darker energy traces, and distinctive visual patterns.For another example, we can consider problems related to VOT, which maybe of concern toa variety of populations. There is a very rich literature regardingthe use of acoustic analyses (both waveform and spectrographic) of VOT-relateddeficits. Individuals who are suffering from aphasia (language deficit due todamage to certain regions of the brain, in this case Broca’s area) seem to beunable to control the timing between the release of a stop and the onset of voic-ing. Individuals with apraxia (neurological disorder of motor programming forspeech) tend to have problems in areas such as timing and coordination thatmight lead to troubles in VOT production. In several studies, people withdysarthria (a group of speech disorders resulting from disease or damage toneural mechanisms that regulate speech movements) have been reported toshow increased variability in their VOT productions. VOT deficiencies havealso been reported in relation to hearing impairment.The significance of VOT deficiencies is not restricted to the clinical contextand is very important in foreign language teaching and accent reductioncontexts. As mentioned earlier in this chapter, and in Chapter 3, the difference144 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTSbetween English /p, t, k/ and /b, d, g/ is not voiced /voiceless in initial position,as /b, d,g/ may also have no voicing before the release of the stop closure. Thus,the aspiration of /p, t, k/ is very important for a segment to be perceived as /p/rather than /b/ (and there is a similar situation for /t/ vs. /d/ and for /k/ vs./g/). Learners of English coming from Romance languages with unaspirated/p, t, k/ (e.g. Spanish, Portuguese) face a big challenge in learning the Englishpatterns, because their productions may be (and indeed, in many cases, are)perceived as /b, d, g/ by native speakers of English. While aspiration of /p, t, k/is perceived by the hearer in a binary fashion (Le. all-or-none occurrence), stud-ies on first and second language acquisition production data show that delay ofthe onset of voicing is typically gradual in the case of voice lag. Thus, the valueof acoustic data becomes ever more important during the remediation process.In considering the productions of the client (learner/ patient), their progressneeds to be constantly evaluated via instrumentation. Monitoring the changesand making the learners or patients aware of this progress, however incom-plete, would encourage them, and thus accelerate the remediation process.It is frequently pointed out that transcribing vowels accurately is more diffi-cult than doing the same for consonants. This task is made harder when we dealwith different vowel sounds that are not part of our native inventory, or differ-ent vowel sounds we might encounter in disordered speech. The key here is toidentify the differences between the system to be remedied and the targetsystem. Although the cardinal vowel system discussed in Chapter 1 may behelpful, it requires a very strong phonetic training if one is to rely on it. Acousticanalysis provides a viable alternative here. As mentioned earlier, vowels can beaccurately described by the frequencies of their first three (in most cases, two)formants. Thus, with the help of spectrographic analysis, the practitioner willbe in a position to identify the nature and the extent of the mismatches betweenthe target system and the system of the patient/student, and plan the remedia-tion accordingly.Apart from its utility in identifying different vowel qualities, acoustic analy-sis is also a powerful tool for vowel durations, an issue that may be crucial inboth a clinical context and foreign language teaching. Here, again, we seefrequent use of waveform analyses as well as spectrographic ones. For example,as we saw earlier, voicing is frequently absent during the closure of final /b, d,g/. Because vowels preceding voiced obstruents are lengthened, the final con-sonant will be perceived as voiced even though no specific evidence of voicingis present. When the learner/patient fails to implement this expected lengthen-ing, the practitioner can identify this unambiguously via spectrographic analy-sis. Also, during remediation she or he can carefully monitor changes in voweldurations via spectrographic data. This can help measure progress by accu-rately describing productions that may not be evident perceptually.Failure to implement voicing contrasts among obstruents is not uncommonin some aphasic patients. For example, a 63-year-old female Broca’s patient(Code and Ball 1982) did not have the voicing contrast involving fricatives (eg.proofing vs. proving, pence vs. pens). Analyzing her data spectrographically,however, the investigators were able to find out that her productions for suchACOUSTICS OF VOWELS AND CONSONANTS 145pairs were not homophonous, and that although she did not have the voiced /voiceless distinctions in target fricatives, she did maintain the contrasts via thelength of the vowel before target fricatives; that is, vowels were (just as innormal speech) longer before voiced (lenis) targets than before voiceless(fortis) targets.The same subject's data were also instructive with respect to the duration oftarget fricatives. As pointed out earlier, the duration of frication of voicelesstargets is longer than that of voiced ones. The subject in this study also made adifference (somewhat smaller than in normal speech) in the duration betweenvoiceless and voiced targets. Thus, out of three parameters that are available tocontrast /f/ with /v/, /s/ with /z/, and so on (voicing of the target, vowellength before the target, duration of frication), the patient was able to utilize thelast two. Without the use of acoustic data, the investigators would not havebeen able to find out that the patient was making the phonemic distinctions andthat the disability was phonetic.All of the above point to the conclusion that acoustic analyses allow a quanti-fication of mismatches between the normative data and the client's produc-tions. In addition to the fact that such quantification is indispensable for thediagnosis of trouble spots, it greatly enhances, by monitoring changes, theability of the professional who deals with the remediation process.Finally, we can also mention that acoustic data can help professionalsdealing with voice disorders, training of the singing voice, speaker identifica-tion, identification of the correlates of speech in stressful conditions, andintoxicated speech.A note on free speech analysis softwareThere are several software packages for speech analysis available free of chargevia the internet. Some of these are simple to use while others are suitable onlyfor those possessing good computing skills. WASP (www.phon.ucLac.uk), andSIL Speech Analyzer (www.sil.org) are easy to use and may be ideal for begin-ners. The former takes up very little disk space, while the latter is capable ofdelivering more functions, and the display format is more flexible. Three morepowerful software packages, however, are Speech Filing System, SES (www.phon.uclac.uk), WAVESURFER (www.speech.kth.se/wavesurfer), and themost widely used PRAAT (www-praat.org).SummaayIn this chapter, we examined acoustic characteristics of speech via spectrogramsand waveforms. Although the precise acoustic makeup of each sound will differfor each individual speaker, there are core features that will enable us to identifygeneral patterns. In the investigation of vowels and diphthongs, the formantpatterns are the cues for recognition. In stops, fricatives, and affricates, whichform the group of obstruents, the different degrees of obstruction reveal clear146 ACOUSTICS OF VOWELS ANDO CONSONANTSacoustic patterns: a gap for stops, frication noise for fricatives, and a stop gapfollowed by a frication noise for affricates. Sonorant consonants behave ratherlike vowels in that they exhibit a voice bar along with formant-like structures inspectrograms, and periodic waves in waveforms. We also saw that the contextin which a particular sound occurs (e.g. adjacent sounds, word positions,suprasegmentals, speech style and rate, etc.) influences the acoustic characteris-tics. We pointed out that spectrograms enable us to differentiate similar soundswithin the major sound classes, while waveforms are useful for durationalmeasurements, and distinguishing major sound classes and voicing differences.Thus, while making measurements, it is advisable to use spectrograms inconjunction with waveforms. Finally, we briefly looked at some practical appli-cations of acoustic analysis in remedial contexts.APPENDIXTips on what to look for when looking at certain sound classes on a spectro-graphic display:StopsVoiced (lenis) vs. voiceless (fortis)# VV #Duration of stop gap voiceless longerVoice bar (/b, d, g/) ?
