Published 2016 by John Wiley & Sons, Lid.Companion website. www.wiley.com/go/yavas/appliedenglishphonology3e282 GLOSSARYaspiration A puff of air following the release of a voiceless stop at the beginning of astressed syllable (e.g. pet [p> et ]).assimilation A process whereby a speech sound is influenced by the surroundingsound(s) to make them more similar (e.g. voicing assimilation of the plural: cats [kets],but dogs [dagz]).Bernoulli effect A drop in air pressure, created by an increase in airflow through aconstriction.bilabial Agound produced with both lips (e.g. /p, b, m/).bisyllabic Containing two syllables. The term disyllabic is a synonym.blade The part of the tongue located just posterior to the tip.body of the tongue The portion of the tongue posterior to the blade.branching onset An onset which contains more than one consonant, as in blue {blul.breathy voice (also murmur} A phonation type in which the vocal cords are onlyslightly apart.broad transcription A transcription that does not show a great deal of phonetic detail;often a simple phonemic transcription.burst Noise created during the release of a stop consonant.cardinal vowels A system of conventional, arbitrarily chosen vowel qualities in termsof which actually occurring vowels may be identified.citation form The form of a word when pronounced in isolation.click A stop sound produced with a velaric ingressive airstream mechanism.closed syllable A syllable that ends in one or more consonant sounds (Le. a syllablewith a coda (e.g. the first syllable of Atlanta, and the last syllable of geminate).co-articulation The overlap of the attributes of successive speech units in articulatoryor acoustic patterns.coda Whatever comes after the nucleus of the syllable (e.g. /t/ in cat).code-switching A phenomenon in which the bilingual speaker switches from onelanguage to another during a single utterance.complementary distribution The allophones of a phoneme are in complementarydistribution in that different allophones occur in different, nonoverlapping, predicta-ble phonological contexts. For example, [0] and [p*] are allophones of the phoneme/p/; the aspirated allophone occurs at the beginning of a stressed syllable and theunaspirated one occurs elsewhere.CON A component of Optimality Theory that provides the criteria (Le. violableconstraints) used to decide between candidates.consonant cluster Two or more adjacent tautosyllabic (in the same syllable) consonants(e.g. sprints has one three-consonant cluster at the beginning and one at the end).content word A word that contributes to the lexical meaning of an utterance; usuallystressed (lexical morphemes such as nouns, verbs, adjectives, adverbs of ime, manner,and place).continuant Segment realized without complete closure of the passage of airflowthrough the oral cavity. Vowels, glides, approximant consonants, and fricatives arecontinuants.contraction Deletion of the vocalic portion of auxiliary verbs and negations that createsthe shortened forms (e.g. Lwill -> 11, will not > won't.contrastive Of a relation between two segments that can occur in the same environ-ment to produce different meanings. From pig and big, we conclude that their initialsounds are contrastive.coronal Consonants which are produced using the blade of the tongue, such as dental,alveolar, and palato-alveolar consonants.GLOSSARY 2883creaky voice (also laryngealization) A type of phonation in which the arytenoid carti-lages hold the posterior end of the vocal cords together so that they can vibrate only atthe other end.ericoid cartilage The posterior cartilage of the larynx, shaped like a signet ring.cv syllable Asyllable consisting of a single consonant in the onset and a single vowelin the nucleus. (e.g. sofa [so.fa]).devoiced A normally voiced sound produced without vocal cord vibration for part ofits duration.diacritic Asmall mark placed to a phonetic symbol which is used to modify the usualmeaning of the symbol or supply further phonetic detail. For example, the small raised[4] in [p*] indicates aspiration . Without it, [p], the sound will be unaspirated.diphthong A complex vowel sound in which the tongue moves from one position toanother in the mouth (e.g. /ai/ in buy).distribution The environments in which a speech sound may occur in a givenlanguage.disyllabic Asynonym for bisyliabic.dorsal A term used to describe sounds in which the body of the tongue features (ie.velar and ueular sounds).ejective Astop sound produced with an egressive glottalic airstream.elision A process in which a consonant is left out in order to make the articulationeasier (e.g. fifth is pronounced [fi6] instead of [ff6)).epenthesis The insertion of one or more sounds in the middle of a word (e.g. prince ispronounced with an epenthetic (t], [paints}).epiglottis Acartilaginous structure that protects the larynx from food and drink duringswallowing.EVAL Acomponent of Optimality Theory that chooses the optimal candidate based onthe constraints.flapping A process in which a [E] or [이 is produced as a flap. (e.g. writer and rider areproduced homophonously as [18620].formant Aconcentration of acoustic energy within a particular frequency band, visiblein a spectrogram as a dark horizontal band.fortis Produced with increased muscular tension. Fortis sounds are usually voiceless.free variation Allophone production that is not tied to a particular phoneticenvironment.frequency The quantity representing the number of complete cycles performed by agiven sound wave per unit time, commonly expressed in hertz (Hz).fricative Aconsonant produced witha partial obstruction of the airstream in which theair is pushed through a narrow constriction, resulting ina friction noise (e.g. /f,s, 8/).function word A word that contributes little to the lexical meaning of an utterance;usually unstressed in a sentence (e.g. grammatical morphemes, such as prepositions,determiners, auxiliary verbs, ard so on).fundamental frequency The basic rate of vocal cord vibration.geminate A sequence of two identical segments (e.g. geminate /n/ in Italian nonno).In English, it can occur only at morpheme boundaries (e.g. unknown).GEN Acomponent of Optimality Theory that generates the list of possible outputs orcandidates.glide (or semi-vewel} A vowel-like speech sound that functions as a consonant (e.g.English /j, w/).glottal stop The sound produced by closing the vocal cords tightly, blocking theairstream from the lungs. :eee284 GLOSSARYglottalic airstream mechanism Upward/downward movement of pharynx air by theaction of the glottis; stops produced this way are called ejectives (upward) and implosives(downward).glottis The space between the vocal cords.grapheme A unit in the spelling system of a language. Graphemes are usually distin-guished from letters, as they can contain more than one letter but still act as a singleunit in the system. (¢.g. <sh> grapheme is used for /{/ in English spelling.heavy syllable A syllable that contains a long vowel or diphthong, or a short vowelfollowed by a consonant.heterosyllabic Belonging to separate syllables (e.g. /b/ and /m/ are heterosyllabic insubmarine [sab.ma.rin)).homophones Words that sound the same but are spelled differently (e.g. right ~ write,scene — seen).homorganic Of sounds, having the same place of articulation (e.g. the last two soundsof limp and tent).implosive A stop made with an ingressive glottalic airstream.intervocalic Between vowels (e.g. abbey [zbi]).intonation The pattern of pitch changes in a phrase or a sentence.intraoral pressure The air pressure within the oral cavity, created by a constriction ofthe articulators during production of stop sounds.IPA International Phonetics Association; also stands for the International PhoneticAlphabet.jargon aphasia A type of fluent aphasia in which an individual's speech is incompre-hensible but appears to make sense to the individual.labial Asound produced with the involvement of one or two lips, such as labiodentalsand bilabials.labialization The presence of some degree of lip rounding (e.g. English /5.3/).labio-dental Anarticulation involving the lower lip and the upper front teeth (e.g. /£,v/).labio-velar Anarticulation with the two lips approaching one another, and the back ofthe tongue raised toward the velum (e.g. /w/).laminal An articulation made with the blade of the tongue.larynx The voicebox: a structure made of cartilage, located in the windpipe, containingvocal cords.lateral plosion The release of a stop by lowering the sides of the tongue, as at the endof ladle.lateral Anarticulation in which there is an obstruction in the midline but the airstreamflows over the sides of the tongue (e.g. /1/).lenis Produced with reduced muscular tension. Lenis sounds are usually voiced.liquids Laterals and various kinds of r-sounds.loanword (adaptation) A word from one language which is introduced into another,such as the English words girl-friend and sky-scraper, which have been borrowed byJapanese. Very often, loanwords are forced into the phonotactic patterns of the hostlanguage, as in [gurufurendu] and isukaisukurepal for the two words given above.locus The apparent point of origin of the formants for each place of articulation.loudness The subjective impression of the magnitude of a sound; it is connected withthe amplitude of the waveform of the sound.manner of articulation The way in which the airstream is modified as it passes throughthe vocal tract in the production of consonants.maximal onset principle Aprinciple which states thata consonant which is equallywell formed in a coda of a syllable and an onset for the following syllable issyllabified as an onset. For example, the syllabification of the word approve isGLOSSARY 985[e.pauv] rather than [ap.suv], despite the fact that the latter can also satisfy the pho-notactic constraints of English, since a coda containing only a /p/ is allowed (e.gcap {kep)}). 님minimal pair A set of two words that differ in one phoneme (e.g. chip - tip [thip] - [trp]).monomorphemic words Words that consist of a single morpheme, such as cat.monophthong A vowel consisting of one distinct articulatory element (cf. diphthong)morpheme The smallest meaningful unit in language. Cat has one morpheme, cats hastwo, unfaithful has three, and unfaithfulness has four morphemes. amurmur See breathy voice.narrow phonetic transcription Transcription of speech where many noncontrastive(vedundant/ predictable) phonetic details are included.nasal plosion The release of a stop by lowering the velum, so that the air escapesthrough the nose (e.g. the end of sudden). Pnasal Asound produced with a lowered velum so that, when the closure in the mouthis released, the air rushes out through the nose as well as through the mouth (e.g. theinitial and final sounds of moon). |nasalized vowel A vowel that is produced with lowered velum so that air escapessimultaneously through the oral cavity and the nasal cavity.neutralization A phonological contrast is said to be neutralized in a specific context ifthe contrast is not attested in that context. For example English /t/ and /d/ contrastin many environments. (02 - dip, bet - bed). In intervocalic position in an unstressedReal contrast is not attested, and thus betting and bedding are both pronouncedas [bere].Northern Cities Shift Achange shift in the sound of some vowels in the dialect regionof American English known as the inland North.nucleus The most prominent part (peak) of a syllable; most often a vowel or adiphthong.obstruent A sound that is articulated with an obstruction in the vocal tract, which isenough to produce friction noise (e.g. stops, fricatives, and affricates).onset The components of a syllable preceding the rhyme (e.g. /bl/ of blue).open syllable A-syllable with no consonant sound at the end (e.g. bee).Optimality Theory (OT) A linguistic model proposing that the observed forms of lan-guage arise from the interaction between conflicting constraints.oral Sounds produced with a closed velopharyngeal passage by raising the velum.palatalization Asecondary articulation involving the raising of the front of the tonguetoward the palate.palato-alveolar An articulation between the tongue blade and the back of the alveolarridge (e.g. /f3/).paraphasia A type of language output error commonly associated with aphasia, andcharacterized by the production of unintended syllables, words, or phrases during theeffort to speak.penult The next-to-last syllable in a word (e.g. pho.no.lo.gy).pharyngealization Asecondary articulation in which the root of the tongue is retractedinto the pharynx.pharynx The part of the vocal tract immediately above the larynx.phonetics The study of speech sounds.phonology The description of patterns of sounds in a language.phonotactics A set of constraints of the possible sequences of phonemes within asyllable or a word.pitch The perceptual correlate of the frequency of a sound. The higher the frequency ofvocal cord vibration is, the higher the pitch.Ce ees286 GLOSSARYplace of articulation Location of the constriction in the vocal tract in production ofaconsonant.polymorphemic words Words that consist of more than one morpheme, such as cats.polysyllabic Containing more than two syllables (e.g. probability).postvocalic Aconsonant following a vowel.prevoicing The onset of voicing before the appearance of a supraglottal articulatoryevent. Also called voicing lead.prosody See suprasegimentals.pulmonic airstream mechanism The movement of lung air by the respiratory muscles.reduced vowel A vowel that is pronounced with a centralized schwa ([ 히 ) quality (e.g.the first vowel of photography and the second vowel of photograph).resonance The way in which the body of air in the vocal tract will vibrate when set inmotion.retracted Produced slightly further back in the vocal tract. For example, the vowel infeel is slightly more retracted than the vowel in feet.retroflex An articulation involving the tip of the tongue and the back of the alveolarridge.thotic Referring to a dialect in which / r/ is pronounced in any position of occurrence.rhyme The partofa syllable that follows the onset (e.g. /int/ in print).Romance language Language historically derived from the evolution of Latin in agiven geographical area. Spanish, Italian, French, Portuguese, Catalan, and Romanian.are among the most important Romance languages.secondary articulation Any articulation that accompanies another (primary) articula-tion and that involves a less radical constriction than the primary articulation, such aspalatalization or labialization.segment An individual sound, consonant, or vowel.semi-vowel See glide.sibilant A fricative or an affricate that is produced with a high-frequency energy, usurally by means of a groove in the tongue (e.g. /s, z, J, 3, U d3/).sociophonetics Phonetic realizations that vary as a function of a range of social factors,such as age, gender, ethnicity, class, and individual identity.sonorant Asound that is not an obstruent (nasals, liquids, glides, vowels).sonority hierarchy A hierarchy of classes of speech sounds organized according totheir degree of sonority in relation to their degree of opening and voicing. Accordingly,vowels are at the top, obstruents are at the bottom, and sonorant consonants are in themiddle of the scale.Sonority Sequencing Principle ‘The idea that the nucleus of a syllable is preceded andfollowed by segments with progressively decreasing sonority in the onset and thecoda, respectively. .Southern Shift A change shift in the sounds of some vowels throughout the Southernstates, the mid-Atlantic states, and the Southern mountain states of the USA.spectrogram A picture of a sound showing how the component frequencies changewith time.spectrum A visual display of the relative amplitudes of the different frequency compo-nents of a sound such as a vowel.spoonerism A kind of speech error involving the transposition of segments, syllables,or words (after the Reverend A. W. Spooner). For example “You have hissed mymystery lecture” instead of “You have missed my history lecture.”stop Aconsonant sound that is produced with a complete closure in some part of thevocal tract, followed by an abrupt release (e.g. /P, d,k/).GLOSSARY 287stress Emphasis ona particular syllable established by |2 loudness, grec ihigher pitch, ¥ greater duration, andstress-timing The rhythm in which stressed syllables occ iee 5 tur at approximately equalsubglottal pressure The air pressure from the |eee 2 e lungs necessary to blow the vocal cordssuprasegmentals Phonetic features that apply to units greater than segments (iesyllables, phrases, sentences), such as stress, length, tone, and intonation ~syllabic consonant A consonant that, in a particular ca i〉 se, functions inucleus (e.g. /n/ in button). 06 a syllablesyllable-timing The rhythm in which each syllable has a i후 roximat iand duration. me Sly equal weighttautosyllabic Refers to segments that belong to the same syllable. For example, /mp/in temptation is tautosyllabic, but is not in complain. camethyroid cartilage The most anterior cartila ige of the larynx to whech ¥ ich the vocal cordstone language A language in which pitch differences function to differentiate wordstone A particular pitch that affects the meaning of a word. |tongue height Refers to the height of the tongue to classify vowel production along thehigh-low dimension in the oral cavity.tonic accent The emphasis given to the tonic syllable in any tone group.tonic syllable The syllable that carries the major pitch change.trill A consonant sound where the active articulator vibrates rapidly striking thepassive articulator repeatedly. (e.g. Spanish perro [pero}).trisyllabic Containing three syllables (e.g. elephant).ult The last syllable in a word (e.g. ra.di.o).noular An articulation involving the back of the tongue and the uvula.velar An articulation involving the back of the tongue and the velum (e.g. /k, g/).velaric airstream mechanism Movement of the mouth air by action of the tongue, asin clicks. ‘velarization Raising the back of the tongue toward the velum, asin the dark /I/ (e.g. full).vocal tract The three resonance chambers taken together: the oral cavity, the nasal cavityand the pharynx.vecoid A sound produced without stoppage or obstruction of the flow of air in thevocal tract; vowel-like.voice bar A dark area near the baseline in a spectrogram, indicating voicing in aconsonant.voice onset time The interval between the release of a stop and the start of voicing forthe following segment.voiced Refers to sounds made with the vocal cords vibrating (e.g. /b, d, m, w, 2/).voiceless Refers to sounds made without vocal cord vibration (e.g. /p, 5 k, f, 8/).vowel quadrilateral A two-dimensional figure that displays the relative position of thetongue (height, and advancement) during vowel production.vowel reduction A process whereby the full form of a vowel is produced shorter andmore centrally (schwa or schwa-like).waveform A graph of changes in amplitude (air pressure) over time.weak form The common, unstressed form of a function word (e.g. [dat] for that).ReferencesAntoniou, M., Best, C.
